*Author :*

m. Bendib nazim

*Supervised by :*

m. Baghdadi mohamed Riyadh (nYUAD)

*Defended on June 26, 2024, before the jury composed of :*

Promotion : 2023/2024

## **Dedication**

I dedicate this work to my mother, who raised me with love, strength, and unwavering support, making me the person I am today. To my father, whose passion for science and insatiable thirst for knowledge inspired my own journey, I owe my deepest gratitude. This work is also dedicated to my beloved brother and sister, whose presence and support have been invaluable.

I would like to extend my heartfelt thanks to all my family members, especially my aunt Fareha N., who has been like a second mother to me, providing guidance and love throughout my life.

To my friends and everyone who has supported me along the way, including family, friends, and teachers, your encouragement has been instrumental in my achievements.

A special dedication goes to Rahim, Islam, Anis, and Raouf, my dear little cousins, who bring joy and inspiration to my life.

**TP T**iling \+ **P**arallelization [49](#of-an-operation,-or-finishing-the-schedule-of-the-current-operation-and-moving-to-the-next-one.-in-our-method,-we-focus-on-the-following-transformation-functions-to-optimize-mlir-code:-tiling-\(t\),-tiling-+-parallelization-\(tp\),-interchange,-and-vectorization.-we-also-eventually-include-fusion-for-a-second-set-of-experiments-where-we-optimize-full-mlir-programs-by-attempting-to-fuse-multiple-operations-together.), [50](#unrolled-action-space), [53](#store-access-matrix-shape:-d-x-\(n-+-1\);-because-we-have-only-one-store-matrix-of-shape-d-x-\(n-+-1\).), [68](#algorithm-7-update-model)

# **Abstract** {#abstract}

Code optimization at the compiler level is crucial for enhancing performance metrics such as speed and memory usage. Traditionally, this task has relied on manual tuning by experts, a process that is both time-consuming and requires significant domain-specific knowledge. Recent advancements have explored automating this process using algorithms and heuristics, with Reinforcement Learning (RL) emerging as a particularly promising approach due to its successes in diverse fields such as robotics and biology. Despite its potential, applying RL to compiler optimization presents challenges, including the need for specialized systems, effective state representations, suitable reward functions, and scalable RL agents.

In this work, we present an RL-based solution for optimizing code within the Multi- Level Intermediate Representation (MLIR) compiler. MLIR, a versatile infrastructure designed to enhance the performance of machine learning models and other computa- tionally intensive applications, is increasingly adopted within the AI community. Our approach introduces a novel formulation of the optimization search space called the Hi- erarchical Action Space, which represents the search space as the Cartesian product of smaller subspaces. Accompanying this is a multi-action policy network, the Hierarchi- cal Policy Network, which efficiently explores the extensive action spaces of potential optimization schedules.

We also developed a comprehensive RL ecosystem for MLIR code optimization and implemented a Python API to facilitate experimental research in this area. Various con- figurations were explored, including an Unrolled Action Space and two distinct reward functions: "Immediate Reward," providing feedback after each optimization, and "Fi- nal Reward," evaluating the entire optimization schedule’s performance. Extensive ex- perimental results demonstrate the efficiency of our method, significantly outperforming traditional heuristics and offering a substantial improvement in both individual MLIR operation optimization and full MLIR code optimization.

# **Résumé** {#résumé}

L’optimisation du code au niveau du compilateur est cruciale pour améliorer des métriques de performance telles que la vitesse et l’utilisation de la mémoire. Tradition- nellement, cette tâche repose sur des ajustements manuels par des experts, un proces- sus à la fois chronophage et nécessitant des connaissances spécifiques au domaine. Des avancées récentes ont exploré l’automatisation de ce processus en utilisant des algorithmes et des heuristiques, avec l’apprentissage par renforcement (RL) émergeant comme une ap- proche particulièrement prometteuse grâce à ses succès dans des domaines divers tels que la robotique et la biologie. Malgré son potentiel, l’application du RL à l’optimisation des compilateurs présente des défis, notamment la nécessité de systèmes spécialisés, de représentations d’état efficaces, de fonctions de récompense appropriées et d’agents RL évolutifs.

Dans ce travail, nous présentons une solution basée sur le RL pour l’optimisation du code au sein du compilateur Multi-Level Intermediate Representation (MLIR). MLIR, une infrastructure polyvalente conçue pour améliorer les performances des modèles d’apprentissage automatique et d’autres applications intensives en calcul, est de plus en plus adop-  
tée au sein de la communauté de l’IA. Notre approche introduit une nouvelle formu- lation de l’espace de recherche d’optimisation appelée l’Espace d’Action Hiérarchique, qui représente l’espace de recherche comme le produit cartésien de sous-espaces plus petits. Cela s’accompagne d’un réseau de politique multi-action, le Réseau de Politique Hiérar- chique, qui explore efficacement les vastes espaces d’action des programmes d’optimisation potentiels.

Nous avons également développé un écosystème complet de RL pour l’optimisation du code MLIR et mis en œuvre une API Python pour faciliter la recherche expérimen- tale dans ce domaine. Diverses configurations ont été explorées, notamment un Espace d’Action Déroulé et deux fonctions de récompense distinctes : "Récompense Immédiate," fournissant un retour d’information après chaque optimisation, et "Récompense Finale," évaluant la performance de l’ensemble du programme d’optimisation. Les résultats ex- périmentaux approfondis démontrent l’efficacité de notre méthode, surpassant de manière significative les heuristiques traditionnelles et offrant une amélioration substantielle tant dans l’optimisation des opérations individuelles de MLIR que dans l’optimisation complète du code MLIR.

# **General introduction** {#general-introduction}

Code optimization is a field of computer science that deals with optimizing code at the compiler level to boost its performance, whether in terms of speed, memory usage, or other metrics depending on the task. Traditionally, code optimization has been performed manually by compiler experts who tune configurations through trial and error to find the best sequence of optimizations to apply to the code. This process is very time-consuming and requires extensive domain-specific knowledge, which makes it even less practical. Consequently, a lot of research has aimed at automating this process using algorithms and heuristics, achieving remarkable results. Among these methods, Reinforcement Learning (RL) has emerged as a promising solution to this problem, given its notable successes in fields such as robotics, games, and biology. Researchers have thus begun exploring the use of RL for code optimization.

However, one major barrier that researchers have faced is that RL requires whole systems to train, which is technically challenging to build for compilers as it requires extensive expertise in both fields to create an efficient experimental environment. Addi- tional RL-specific challenges also arise, such as determining the best state representation, identifying the most effective reward function, and training scalable RL agents.

The goal of this project is to develop an RL-based solution to tackle code optimization for the MLIR compiler. MLIR, which stands for Multi-Level Intermediate Representation, is a compiler infrastructure designed to facilitate the development of high-performance machine learning models, as well as other computationally intensive applications. It was originally developed by Google and has since become an open-source project within the LLVM ecosystem. This project is promising because the AI community is increasingly adopting MLIR as a code compiler for scientific code, machine learning algorithms, and deep learning architectures. So optimizing code in the MLIR compiler could significantly benefit the entire AI community.

This thesis manuscript is divided into three parts. The first part is a related work chapter, where we discuss the main concepts in code optimization and RL, as well as related research that has explored using RL for this field. The second part covers the de- sign and implementation of our solution, detailing our RL-MLIR environment, RL policy networks, and the RL algorithms that we used. Finally, we present the results obtained through extensive experimentation divided into two sets: first optimizing individual MLIR operations, and then optimizing full MLIR codes.

# **Chapter 1** {#chapter-1}

**Code Optimization**

1. ## **Introduction** {#introduction}

Code optimization is a fundamental practice in software development. It involves en- hancing the performance, efficiency, and resource utilization of the code by refining the intermediate code while reducing resource consumption (such as CPU and memory). As nowadays problems are becoming more complex, code optimization becomes crucial for achieving better results and meeting specific requirements. The compiler optimization process should maintain correctness, enhance the speed and overall performance of the program and should be kept within reasonable limits.

Traditional manual compiler code optimization involves developers manually inspect- ing and modifying source code to enhance its efficiency. This process requires a deep understanding of the underlying hardware architecture, instruction sets, and low-level programming techniques. Programmers identify and implement optimizations, such as loop unrolling, instruction scheduling, and memory management, to improve perfor- mance. This approach demands significant expertise in code optimization and can be time-consuming. Nowadays, developers rely on more sophisticated ways to perform code optimization. Most modern compilers automatically perform several transformations that would be tedious to do manually and would make the source code less readable, even though it may be necessary in very specific cases. They leverage sophisticated compiler technologies to enhance code performance without manual intervention using various op- timization techniques like inlining functions, reordering instructions, and applying al- gorithmic improvements automatically. These tools analyze program behavior, identify bottlenecks, and generate optimized machine code. While automatic optimization stream- lines the development process, it may not always achieve the same level of fine-tuning as manual optimization, so striking a balance between manual and automatic approaches is crucial for achieving optimal performance in software development.

2. ## **Machine-Dependent vs Machine-Independent Op- timization** {#machine-dependent-vs-machine-independent-op--timization}

According to (Thomas, [2004](#ers.)), we have two types of code optimizations: (1) Machine- Dependent Optimization that tailors the code on very specific hardware, and (2) Machine- Independent Optimization that optimizes code and makes it more efficient on all kinds of hardware.

1. ### **Machine-Dependent Optimization** {#machine-dependent-optimization}

Machine-dependent optimization refers to the practice of performing code optimizations with respect to the specific characteristics and architecture of the target machine or hard- ware platform. Machine-dependent optimization can be applied at various levels of the compilation chain, from the source code to the generated machine code (Thomas, [2004](#ers.)). It typically involves low-level optimizations that leverage specific features of the hardware ar- chitecture to enhance code performance. Common techniques used for machine-dependent optimization include the use of architecture-specific compilation directives, optimization of assembly code, cache management optimization, etc. This optimization approach is particularly useful for programs targeting a specific hardware platform that needs to achieve optimal performance on that platform. It allows leveraging specific features of the hardware architecture and optimizing the code for optimal utilization of the targeted platform. In most compute-intensive programs, loops and functions often account for the majority of the computation time (Song et al., [2019](#shpeisman,-t.,-bondhugula,-a.,-lattner,-c.,-et-al.-\(2021\).-mlir:-a-compiler-infrastructure-for-the-end-of-moore’s-law.-2021-ieee/acm-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization-\(cgo\),-2–14.-https://doi.org/10.1109/cgo51591.-2021.9370308)). Therefore, it is essential to explore code transformations that specifically target these elements.

2. ### **Machine-Independent  Optimization** {#machine-independent-optimization}

Machine-independent optimization is a code optimization approach aimed at improving the performance of a program without considering the specific characteristics of a given hardware architecture (Thomas, [2004](#ers.)). In contrast to machine-dependent optimization, which takes into account the features of a specific processor, machine-independent op- timization can be applied at various levels of the compilation chain, from the source code to the generated machine code. Common techniques used for machine-independent optimization include algorithmic optimization, involving modifications to the underlying algorithm to enhance performance, and data structure optimization, involving the reor- ganization of data to facilitate processing. Other techniques include loop optimization (loop permutation, loop unrolling, etc.), elimination of intermediate variables, vectoriza- tion, and function call optimization. Machine-independent optimization is particularly useful for multi-platform programs or applications that need to run on different hardware architectures (TPU, GPU, CPU). By employing this approach, developers can enhance the performance of their programs on various platforms without needing to focus on the specificities of each one.

3. ### **Balance Between Machine-Dependent and Machine-Independent** {#balance-between-machine-dependent-and-machine-independent}

The choice between machine-dependent and machine-independent optimization is a nu- anced decision influenced by factors such as the target audience, platform diversity, and performance requirements. While machine-dependent optimization offers the potential for highly tuned performance on a specific hardware platform, it comes at the cost of reduced portability. On the other hand, machine-independent optimization prioritizes portability but may not exploit the full potential of specific hardware features.

In practice, developers often navigate a middle ground, employing a hybrid approach that combines elements of both machine-dependent and machine-independent optimiza- tion (Song et al., [2019](#shpeisman,-t.,-bondhugula,-a.,-lattner,-c.,-et-al.-\(2021\).-mlir:-a-compiler-infrastructure-for-the-end-of-moore’s-law.-2021-ieee/acm-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization-\(cgo\),-2–14.-https://doi.org/10.1109/cgo51591.-2021.9370308)). This allows them to strike a balance, achieving optimal per- formance across a variety of platforms while leveraging specific optimizations for critical hardware scenarios. Ultimately, the choice depends on the specific goals, constraints, and characteristics of the software project at hand.

3. ## **Profiling and Performance Analysis** {#profiling-and-performance-analysis}

Profiling and performance analysis are crucial aspects of code optimization, helping de- velopers identify bottlenecks, inefficiencies, and areas for improvement in their software. These practices are integral to enhancing the overall performance of a program, ensuring it runs efficiently and meets the desired speed and resource utilization criteria.

1. ### **Profiling** {#profiling}

Profiling is the process of measuring various aspects of a program’s execution to under- stand its behavior and resource usage. Profiling tools provide insights into how much time and resources each function or section of code consumes during execution. This in- formation helps developers pinpoint performance bottlenecks and focus their optimization efforts on the most critical areas (Cooper & Torczon, [2011](#cho,-k.,-van-merrienboer,-b.,-gulcehre,-c.,-bahdanau,-d.,-bougares,-f.,-schwenk,-h.,-&-bengio,-y.-\(2014\).-learning-phrase-representations-using-rnn-encoder-decoder-for-statistical-machine-translation.-proceedings-of-the-2014-conference-on-empirical-methods-in-natural-language-processing-\(emnlp\),-1724–1734.)). There are two main types of profiling:

#### **Time Profiling**

* **CPU Time Profiling:** Identifies the amount of CPU time each function consumes. This is crucial for identifying functions that consume a significant portion of the overall execution time.

  * **Wall Time Profiling:** Measures the actual time elapsed during the execution of the program, including time spent waiting for external resources.

#### **Memory Profiling**

* **Heap Profiling:** Analyzes dynamic memory allocation and deallocation, helping identify memory leaks and inefficient memory usage.

  * **Stack Profiling:** Examines the stack memory to understand function call sequences and memory consumption.

    2. ### **Performance Analysis** {#performance-analysis}

Performance analysis involves a broader examination of a program’s overall efficiency and resource utilization. Unlike profiling, performance analysis also considers factors such as algorithmic complexity, I/O operations, and external dependencies. Performance analysis aims to provide a comprehensive view of the software’s behavior and highlights areas for improvement. Key aspects of performance analysis include:

* **Algorithmic Complexity Analysis:** Identifying inefficient algorithms that result in poor scalability and increased resource usage.

  * **I/O Operations Analysis:** Evaluating the impact of input and output operations on the overall performance, especially in scenarios with large data sets.

    * **External Dependency Analysis:** Assessing the impact of external libraries or services on the program’s performance.

  4. ## **Loop Nest Optimization** {#loop-nest-optimization}

Researchers and developers have discovered that the most resource-intensive component of code, particularly in scientific applications, is the loop nest. Consequently, more focus was put on optimizing loop nests, as this yields the most significant efficiency improvements.

In compiler theory, loop optimization is a crucial process for enhancing execution speed and reducing costs associated with loops (Thomas, [2004](#ers.)). It plays a significant role in optimizing cache management and the efficient utilization of parallel processing capabilities. However, loop optimization is complex due to the repetitive execution of instructions within loops and their high level of dependency, making it challenging to find the most optimal transformations to apply on a loop nest along with their order.

In the following, we will introduce some of the most used loop transformations in the literature:

1. ### **Parallelization** {#parallelization}

Parallelization involves the identification and exploitation of parallelism within the code, enabling the simultaneous execution of multiple iterations within a loop nest (Bacon et

Figure 1.1: A diagram demonstrating the effect of parallelization on loop iterations.

al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)). This transformation technique results in parallel loops, executed concurrently by multiple threads or processes on parallel processors. The execution of the same code region co-occurs with different data inputs. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Increased Performance by executing loops in parallel.

  * Better resource exploitation.

    * Increased scalability as it enables running larger computations.

    2. ### **Interchange (Permutation)** {#interchange-(permutation)}

Loop interchange, also known as loop permutation, is a loop transformation that reorders nested loops to improve data locality and enhance parallelism (Bacon et al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)). The primary goal of loop interchange is to optimize memory access patterns and potentially increase cache efficiency. By swapping the order of nested loops, developers aim to im- prove the reuse of data in the cache, thereby reducing cache misses and enhancing overall program performance. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Improved Data Locality

  * Parallelism Opportunities

    * Optimized Computation

    3. ### **Tiling** {#tiling}

Loop tiling is a crucial technique in compiler optimization aimed at improving cache locality and overall program performance (Bacon et al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)). This method involves dividing a loop into smaller, regular-sized blocks called tiles, and then iterating over these tiles. The primary goal of loop tiling is to enhance data locality by ensuring that the data accessed within each tile remains in the cache, reducing cache misses and improving memory access efficiency. The main advantages can be summarized in:  
![][image2]

Figure 1.2: A diagram demonstrating the effect of interchange on loop nests.

* Enhanced Cache Usage

  * Parallelism Opportunities

    * Improved Data Reuse

Figure 1.3: A diagram demonstrating the effect of tiling on loop nests.

4. ### **Fusion** {#fusion}

Loop fusion is a transformation technique that combines multiple loops into a single, more efficient loop structure (Bacon et al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)). The primary objective of loop fusion is to reduce loop control overhead and improve cache locality by consolidating the execution of multiple loops into a unified loop. This transformation aims to eliminate redundant loop control operations and enhance data reuse, thereby reducing memory access latency and improving overall program performance. Loop fusion is particularly advantageous when dealing with consecutive loops that iterate over the same data sets, as it streamlines the computation and potentially reduces cache misses. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Reduced Loop Overhead

  * Cache Locality

    * Simplified Code

![][image3]

Figure 1.4: A diagram demonstrating the effect of fusion on loop nests.

5. ### **Vectorization** {#vectorization}

Loop vectorization is a key optimization technique employed in compiler design to ex- ploit Single Instruction, Multiple Data (SIMD) capabilities of modern processors. This process transforms scalar operations within a loop into vector operations, allowing the processor to perform the same operation on multiple data elements simultaneously. By exploiting SIMD instructions, loop vectorization enhances parallelism, thereby accelerat- ing the execution of the loop and improving overall program performance. This technique is especially beneficial when dealing with data-parallel computations, such as those found in numerical simulations or signal-processing tasks. The main advantages can be summa- rized in:

* SIMD Utilization

  * Enhanced Parallelism

    * Enhanced Parallelism

    6. ### **Skewing** {#skewing}

Loop skewing involves altering the loop index variables to create a more regular access pattern, thereby enhancing the potential for parallelism and improving overall program performance. By introducing a skewing factor into the loop indices, developers aim to balance the workload across iterations and reduce dependencies, making it easier to exploit parallel processing capabilities. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Balanced Workload

  * Reduced Dependencies

    * Optimized Data Access

    7. ### **Loop Unswitching** {#loop-unswitching}

Loop unswitching improves the performance of loops by moving conditional branches outside the loop body (Bacon et al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)). This transformation is particularly useful when  
![][image4]

Figure 1.5: A diagram demonstrating the effect of skewing on loop nests.

a loop contains a conditional statement that does not change within the loop iterations, resulting in repeated evaluations of the same condition. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Elimination of Redundant Evaluations

  * Improved Code Clarity

    * Potential for Further Optimizations

  Figure 1.6: A diagram demonstrating the effect of loop unswitching on loop nests.

    8. ### **Inversion** {#inversion}

Loop inversion involves restructuring a loop to invert the dependencies within its body. The loop is modified to execute in the opposite direction, changing the way loop indices are incremented or decremented (Bacon et al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)). The primary goal of loop inversion is to expose opportunities for parallelism and enhance cache locality by altering the order in which data elements are accessed. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Parallelism Opportunities

  * Cache Locality

    * Optimized Data Access

![][image5]

Figure 1.7: A diagram demonstrating the effect of inversion on loop nests.

9. ### **Unrolling** {#unrolling}

Loop unrolling aims at improving the efficiency of loops by reducing the loop control over- head and exploiting instruction-level parallelism (Thomas, [2004](#ers.)). In this transformation, the compiler replicates the loop body’s contents, effectively increasing the granularity of computation within each iteration (Cooper & Torczon, [2011](#cho,-k.,-van-merrienboer,-b.,-gulcehre,-c.,-bahdanau,-d.,-bougares,-f.,-schwenk,-h.,-&-bengio,-y.-\(2014\).-learning-phrase-representations-using-rnn-encoder-decoder-for-statistical-machine-translation.-proceedings-of-the-2014-conference-on-empirical-methods-in-natural-language-processing-\(emnlp\),-1724–1734.)). Unrolling a loop allows multiple iterations to be grouped together, enabling more efficient use of available re- sources, such as registers and pipeline stages. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Reduced Loop Control Overhead

  * Improved Instruction Scheduling

    * Enhanced Parallelism

Figure 1.8: A diagram demonstrating the effect of unrolling on loop nests.

10. ### **Loop Peeling** {#loop-peeling}

Loop peeling involves extracting a small number of initial or final iterations from a loop and handling them separately outside the main loop structure. This transformation is typically applied when the loop has boundary conditions or special cases that can be processed more efficiently when isolated. The main advantages can be summarized in:

* Special Case Optimization

  * Reduced Loop Overhead

    * Optimized Code Execution

  Figure 1.9: A diagram demonstrating the effect of loop peeling on loop nests.

  5. ## **Multi-Level Intermediate Representation (MLIR)** {#multi-level-intermediate-representation-(mlir)}

[MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) (Lattner et al., [2021](#lattner,-c.,-&-adve,-v.-\(2004\).-llvm:-a-compilation-framework-for-lifelong-program-analysis-&-transformation.-proceedings-of-the-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization:-feedback-directed-and-runtime-optimization,-75.)) is a compiler infrastructure designed to facilitate the de- velopment of high-performance and portable machine learning models, as well as other computationally intensive applications. It was originally developed by Google and has since become an open-source project within the [Low Level Virtual Machine (LLVM)](#llvm-low-level-virtual-machine-13,-40,-43,-63) (Lattner & Adve, [2004](#krizhevsky,-a.,-sutskever,-i.,-&-hinton,-g.-e.-\(2012\).-imagenet-classification-with-deep-convolutional-neural-networks.-advances-in-neural-information-processing-sys--tems,-1097–1105.)) ecosystem. [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) addresses several challenges in modern com- piler design, particularly those arising from the increasing complexity and diversity of hardware architectures and programming models.

[MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) is built with a highly modular architecture, allowing developers to define and integrate new [Intermediate Representation (IR)](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48)s tailored to specific domains or optimiza- tion needs. Unlike traditional compiler infrastructures that typically use a single [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48), [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) supports multiple levels of [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48), each optimized for different stages of the compilation pro- cess. This hierarchical approach enables fine-grained optimization and transformation at various levels of abstraction, improving both the efficiency and portability of the generated code.

1. ### [**MLIR**](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) **for Machine Learning** {#mlir-for-machine-learning}

[MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,)’s origins in machine learning stem from the need to optimize deep learning mod- els for diverse and evolving hardware architectures. Traditional compiler infrastructures struggled to keep pace with the rapid advancements in machine learning hardware, lead- ing to performance bottlenecks and portability issues. [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) addresses these challenges through its multi-level approach, enabling more efficient compilation and optimization of machine learning models.

[MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) provides specialized dialects for tensor operations, which are fundamental to machine learning workloads. These domain-specific dialects support sophisticated trans- formations and optimizations that are critical for achieving high performance on modern hardware. [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) also facilitates the representation and transformation of computational graphs, which are central to many machine learning frameworks. This capability allows

for advanced graph optimizations, such as operator fusion and scheduling, improving both the speed and efficiency of model execution.

6. ## **Conclusion** {#conclusion}

In this section, we conducted a study on code optimization by exploring various types, such as machine-dependent and machine-independent optimizations. Additionally, we discussed profiling and performance analysis as tools for evaluating code, and we delved into loop nest optimization in detail, underlying the most renowned techniques in the literature with examples. We also introduced [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) (Lattner et al., [2021](#lattner,-c.,-&-adve,-v.-\(2004\).-llvm:-a-compilation-framework-for-lifelong-program-analysis-&-transformation.-proceedings-of-the-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization:-feedback-directed-and-runtime-optimization,-75.)), a promising new compiler framework utilizing [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48)s optimized for specific stages of the compilation process. [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) shows great potential for optimizing scientific code and deep learning architectures.

# **Chapter 2 Reinforcement learning** {#chapter-2-reinforcement-learning}

1. ## **Machine Learning** {#machine-learning}

[machine learning (ML)](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) is a field of [Artificial Intelligence (AI)](#ai-artificial-intelligence-15,-17) that exploits the available data to learn and improve autonomously. Essentially, it equips machines with the ability to extract knowledge and insights from data, allowing them to make predictions, solve problems, and make decisions without explicit programming. This technology is rapidly reshaping various aspects of our world, from healthcare and finance to entertainment and robotics.

1. ### **Types of Machine Learning** {#types-of-machine-learning}

[ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) can be divided into multiple categories, each with its respective paradigm, algorithms, and use cases:

#### **Supervised learning**

Figure 2.1: Supervised learning diagram.

Supervised learning (Bishop, [2006](#baghdadi,-r.,-ray,-j.,-romdhane,-m.-b.,-del-sozzo,-e.,-akkas,-a.,-zhang,-y.,-suriana,-p.,-kamil,-s.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2019\).-tiramisu:-a-polyhedral-compiler-for-ex--pressing-fast-and-portable-code.-2019-ieee/acm-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization-\(cgo\),-193–205.)) is extensively utilized in various fields, including image recognition, speech recognition, and natural language processing, where a clear connection exists between input and output. A model is trained using a labeled dataset, with each input data point paired with a corresponding output or target. The objective

is for the model to learn the mapping between input features and the correct output by generalizing from the labeled examples. Throughout training, the algorithm continually refines its parameters to minimize the gap between its predictions and the actual labeled targets. Some of the famous algorithms are:

* Linear regression.

  * Support Vector Machine (SVM).

    * Decision Trees.

#### **Unsupervised learning**

Figure 2.2: Unsupervised learning diagram.

Unlike supervised learning, unsupervised learning (Hastie et al., [2009](#goodfellow,-i.,-bengio,-y.,-&-courville,-a.-\(2016\).-deep-learning.-mit-press.)) deals with un- labeled data, where the algorithm is tasked with discovering patterns, structures, or rela- tionships within the input data without explicit guidance. Clustering and dimensionality reduction are common tasks in unsupervised learning. Clustering algorithms group simi- lar data points together, while dimensionality reduction techniques aim to represent the data in a lower-dimensional space, simplifying its complexity. Unsupervised learning is crucial for exploring and understanding the inherent structures within data, making it valuable in scenarios where labeled training data is expensive to obtain. Some of the famous algorithms are:

* K-means.

  * DBscan.

    * Association Rules.

#### **Semi-Supervised learning**

Semi-supervised learning (Chapelle et al., [2009](#brockman,-g.,-cheung,-v.,-pettersson,-l.,-schneider,-j.,-schulman,-j.,-tang,-j.,-&-zaremba,-w.-\(2016\).-openai-gym.-arxiv-preprint-arxiv:1606.01540.)) combines elements of both supervised and unsupervised learning. In this approach, the algorithm is trained on a dataset containing both labeled and unlabeled examples. The model leverages the labeled data for supervised learning tasks while also extracting information from the unlabeled data to improve its overall performance. Semi-supervised learning is particularly beneficial when acquiring labeled data is resource-intensive, as it allows the model to learn from both labeled and unlabeled information.  
![][image6]

Figure 2.3: Semi-supervised learning diagram.

#### **Self Supervised learning**

Self-supervised learning (Jing & Tian, [2020](#hochreiter,-s.,-&-schmidhuber,-j.-\(1997\).-long-short-term-memory.-neural-computation,-9-\(8\),-1735–1780.)) is a subset of unsupervised learning where the algorithm generates its own labels from the input data. Instead of relying on external annotations, the task is reformulated so that it can be solved without explicit supervision. These tasks often involve creating surrogate objectives, such as predicting missing parts of the input or generating context from the data itself. Self-supervised learning has gained prominence due to its ability to pre-train models on vast amounts of unlabeled data, subsequently fine-tuning them for specific tasks with limited labeled examples.

Figure 2.4: Self-supervised learning diagram.

#### **Reinforcement learning**

[RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) (Sutton & Barto, [2018](#sutton,-r.-s.-\(1988\).-learning-to-predict-by-the-methods-of-temporal-differences.-machine-learning,-3-\(1\),-9–44.)) is a [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) paradigm where an agent learns to make decisions by interacting with an environment. The agent receives feedback in the form of rewards or penalties based on the actions it takes, allowing it to learn optimal strategies through trial and error. [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) is commonly applied in scenarios where an agent needs to make a sequence of decisions over time, such as playing games, robotic control, and autonomous systems. The agent’s goal is to learn a policy that maximizes cumulative reward, navigating the trade-off between exploration and exploitation to achieve optimal performance in its given environment.

2. ## **Deep Learning** {#deep-learning}

Neural networks (Haykin, [1994](#haykin,-s.-\(1994\).-neural-networks:-a-comprehensive-foundation.-prentice-hall.-hennouni,-n.,-&-el-hassane,-n.-\(2022,-june\).-utilisation-de-l’apprentissage-par-renforce-)) are a fundamental component of [AI](#ai-artificial-intelligence-15,-17) and [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) systems, playing a crucial role in various applications. They are inspired by the structure and functioning of the human brain (Goodfellow et al., [2016](#dubey,-s.-r.,-singh,-s.-k.,-&-chaudhuri,-b.-b.-\(2021\).-a-comprehensive-survey-and-per--formance-analysis-of-activation-functions-in-deep-learning.-corr,-abs/2109.14545.-https://arxiv.org/abs/2109.14545)), consisting of interconnected  
![][image7]

Figure 2.5: Reinforcement learning diagram.

nodes (neurons) organized into layers. The primary objective of neural networks is to learn and generalize patterns from data, enabling them to make predictions, classify in- formation, and perform various tasks.

Neural networks typically consist of three main types of layers (Haykin, [1994](#haykin,-s.-\(1994\).-neural-networks:-a-comprehensive-foundation.-prentice-hall.-hennouni,-n.,-&-el-hassane,-n.-\(2022,-june\).-utilisation-de-l’apprentissage-par-renforce-)):

* **Input Layer:** This layer receives the initial data or features that the network will process.

* **Hidden Layers:** These layers process the input data through weighted connections and activation functions. Multiple hidden layers allow the network to learn complex representations.

* **Output Layer:** This layer produces the final output or prediction based on the processed information from the hidden layers.

  1. ### **Types of Neural Networks** {#types-of-neural-networks}

#### **Feedforward Neural Networks (FNN)**

Feedforward Neural Networks (Goodfellow et al., [2016](#dubey,-s.-r.,-singh,-s.-k.,-&-chaudhuri,-b.-b.-\(2021\).-a-comprehensive-survey-and-per--formance-analysis-of-activation-functions-in-deep-learning.-corr,-abs/2109.14545.-https://arxiv.org/abs/2109.14545); Haykin, [1994](#haykin,-s.-\(1994\).-neural-networks:-a-comprehensive-foundation.-prentice-hall.-hennouni,-n.,-&-el-hassane,-n.-\(2022,-june\).-utilisation-de-l’apprentissage-par-renforce-)), also known as mul- tilayer perceptrons, are a foundational architecture in neural network design. In FNNs, information flows in a unidirectional manner, passing through input, hidden, and output layers. Each connection between nodes carries a weight, and the network learns by adjust- ing these weights during training using the backpropagation algorithm. FNNs are versatile and find application in tasks such as pattern recognition, classification, and regression. However, they may face challenges in capturing sequential dependencies in data due to their lack of internal memory, leading to the development of more advanced architectures like Recurrent Neural Networks.

#### **Convolutional Neural Networks (CNN)**

Convolutional Neural Networks (Krizhevsky et al., [2012](#jing,-l.,-&-tian,-y.-\(2020\).-self-supervised-learning-for-semi-supervised-time-series-clas--sification.-ieee-transactions-on-knowledge-and-data-engineering,-32-\(5\),-997–-1008.)) have revolutionized the field of computer vision and image processing. CNNs are designed to efficiently process grid-like

data, such as images, by employing convolutional layers that automatically learn hierar- chical features. These networks excel at capturing spatial patterns and local correlations, making them highly effective in tasks like image classification, object detection, and facial recognition. The use of convolutional operations allows CNNs to reduce the dimensionality of input data while preserving important features. Transfer learning, where pre-trained CNN models are fine-tuned for specific tasks, has further enhanced their applicability across various domains beyond image analysis, such as natural language processing and medical image diagnosis.

#### **Recurrent Neural Networks ([Recurrent Neural Network (RNN)](#rnn-recurrent-neural-network-19))**

Recurrent Neural Networks (Hochreiter & Schmidhuber, [1997](#ment-dans-l’exploration-de-l’espace-de-recherche-pour-l’optimisation-automatique-des-compilateurs-[master’s-thesis,-école-nationale-supérieure-d’informatique-\(esi-ex-ini\)].)) are designed to address the limitation of capturing sequential information in data. Unlike feedforward networks, RNNs have connections that form loops, allowing them to maintain a memory of previous inputs. This architecture is particularly well-suited for processing sequential data, such as time series, natural language, and speech. RNNs exhibit dynamic temporal behavior, making them adept at tasks where context and order matter. Despite their strengths, traditional RNNs may suffer from vanishing or exploding gradient problems during train- ing. To overcome these issues, more advanced variants, such as Long Short-Term Memory ([Long Short Term Memory (LSTM)](#lstm-long-short-term-memory-19)) (Shivanshu, [2023](#m.-\(2022\).-reinforcement-learning-strategies-for-compiler-optimization-in-high-level-synthesis.-2022-ieee/acm-eighth-workshop-on-the-llvm-compiler-in--frastructure-in-hpc-\(llvm-hpc\),-13–22.-https-:-/-/-doi-.-org-/-10-.-1109-/-llvm---hpc56686.2022.00007)) networks and Gated Recurrent Units (GRU) (Cho et al., [2014](#chapelle,-o.,-scholkopf,-b.,-&-zien,-a.-\(2009\).-semi-supervised-learning.-mit-press.)), have been developed to improve the learning of long-term dependencies.

2. ### **Strengths of Deep Learning** {#strengths-of-deep-learning}

   * **Automatic feature learning:** Unlike traditional [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44), deep learning can automat- ically extract and learn complex features directly from data, eliminating the need for manual feature engineering. This can be especially advantageous for tasks with high-dimensional or complex data (Goodfellow et al., [2016](#dubey,-s.-r.,-singh,-s.-k.,-&-chaudhuri,-b.-b.-\(2021\).-a-comprehensive-survey-and-per--formance-analysis-of-activation-functions-in-deep-learning.-corr,-abs/2109.14545.-https://arxiv.org/abs/2109.14545)).

     * **Superior performance on complex tasks:** Deep learning excels at tasks like image recognition, natural language processing, and speech recognition, often sur- passing the performance of traditional [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) methods. This is due to its ability to capture subtle relationships and non-linearities within data (Sutton, [1988](#1919-7)).

       * **Handling unstructured data:** Deep learning can effectively handle unstructured data like images, text, and audio, whereas traditional methods often require feature extraction to convert them into structured formats. This opens up possibilities for diverse applications like computer vision and language translation (Goodfellow et al., [2016](#dubey,-s.-r.,-singh,-s.-k.,-&-chaudhuri,-b.-b.-\(2021\).-a-comprehensive-survey-and-per--formance-analysis-of-activation-functions-in-deep-learning.-corr,-abs/2109.14545.-https://arxiv.org/abs/2109.14545); Shivanshu, [2023](#m.-\(2022\).-reinforcement-learning-strategies-for-compiler-optimization-in-high-level-synthesis.-2022-ieee/acm-eighth-workshop-on-the-llvm-compiler-in--frastructure-in-hpc-\(llvm-hpc\),-13–22.-https-:-/-/-doi-.-org-/-10-.-1109-/-llvm---hpc56686.2022.00007)).

       * **Improved accuracy with large data:** Deep learning algorithms benefit sig- nificantly from increased data availability. With large datasets, they can achieve high levels of accuracy and model complex relationships with greater precision (Krizhevsky et al., [2012](#jing,-l.,-&-tian,-y.-\(2020\).-self-supervised-learning-for-semi-supervised-time-series-clas--sification.-ieee-transactions-on-knowledge-and-data-engineering,-32-\(5\),-997–-1008.)).

       * **Scalability for real-world problems:** Deep learning models can be efficiently scaled to handle larger datasets and tasks with increased complexity. This allows them to be applied to real-world problems with significant impact.

Finally, it’s also important to note that deep learning also has limitations, such as requiring more computational resources and data, and often lacking interpretability com- pared to traditional methods. Choosing the right approach depends on the specific task and its data characteristics.

3. ## **Reinforcement learning** {#reinforcement-learning}

[RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) involves teaching an agent what to do—how to take the right actions from particular states—by maximizing a reward in the form of a numerical signal (Sutton, [1988](#1919-7); Sutton & Barto, [2018](#sutton,-r.-s.-\(1988\).-learning-to-predict-by-the-methods-of-temporal-differences.-machine-learning,-3-\(1\),-9–44.)). The agent is not informed in advance about how to choose actions to solve the task optimally; it must discover them through trial and error.

In this chapter, we will detail the main components of a [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system and the techniques used to adapt it to different types of problems.

4. ## **Components of a Reinforcement Learning System** {#components-of-a-reinforcement-learning-system}

A [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system consists of two main interacting components: an environment and an agent (Sutton & Barto, [2018](#sutton,-r.-s.-\(1988\).-learning-to-predict-by-the-methods-of-temporal-differences.-machine-learning,-3-\(1\),-9–44.)). The agent performs an action in the environment, receiving in return the reward for that action and a new state to reach.

* **State:** It contains information about the environment’s properties in the current state, such as the agent’s position, present objects, etc. It can be of any format: a vector, an image, a graph, etc. . .

* **Action:** The transition leading the agent from state S*t* to state S*t*\+1. This transition is rewarded by a value R*t* as the environment’s response to this action.

* **Reward:** This numerical signal, R*t*, is sent by the environment and received by the agent, indicating the result of the last chosen action A*t*. The total cumulative rewards accumulated by the agent through a sequence of actions is referred to as the gain or accumulated return, denoted as G*t*.

* **Episode:** A sequence of states, actions, and rewards that begins from an initial state and continues until a terminal state is reached.

* **Policy:** it is a function that affects a probability of selection π(a|s) to a state-action pair (s, a). There are two types of policies: Deterministic policy that maps each state directly to a specific action; and Stochastic policy that defines a probability distribution over actions for each state. We refer to the best obtainable policy by

  Optimal Policy, which is a policy that maximizes the expected cumulative reward for an agent in a given environment.

* **Value Functions:** There are two types of value functions, (1) State value V (S*t*) that represents the expected cumulative reward that we can obtain starting from a state S*t*, and (2) State-action value V (S*t*|A*t*) that represents the expected cumulative reward that we can obtain by taking action A*t* from a state S*t*.

It is the agent’s responsibility to estimate the value of states and actions to obtain an optimal policy. The fundamental elements for a [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent are reward, state and state- action value functions, policy, and the model (if it exists) describing the environment.

5. ## **Types of Reinforcement Learning Algorithms** {#types-of-reinforcement-learning-algorithms}

Modeling a [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) problem is a key step in solving the problem. It involves defining state and action spaces, transition and reward functions, selecting the learning method, training the agent, and evaluating its performance. We can classify a [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) problem using three criteria:  
(1) The size of the state and action spaces; (2) The complexity of the agent’s environment;

(3) The choice of policy training type between online and offline.

Figure 2.6: A taxonomy of reinforcement learning algorithms.

1. ### **Tabular Approach vs Approximate Approach** {#tabular-approach-vs-approximate-approach}

Two main approaches exist in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) for representing the value of a state or action: tabular approach and approximate approach.

1) **Tabular:** Simple and effective for small environments. Uses a two-dimensional table where each cell in the table stores the value of a specific state or action.

2) **Approximate:** More suitable for complex environments. Uses a function (usually a neural network) to estimate the value function, resulting in a more compact rep- resentation. For instance, a neural network would be better for a complex game like Go than a large table.

   2. ### **Model Based vs Model Free** {#model-based-vs-model-free}

Based on the underlying representation of the environment’s dynamics (distinct from the concept of a "model" in [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44)), [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) (RL) can be classified into two primary paradigms:

1) **Model-based:** Relies on a transition function P(s′|s, a) to predict the probability of transitioning from one state s to another state s′ based on a chosen action a. This approach can be inefficient due to the difficulty of accurately modeling complex environments.

2) **Model-free:** Doesn’t require a transition function as environments are often stochas- tic. Uses **Temporal Difference (TD)** and **Monte Carlo (MC)** (Sutton & Barto, [2018](#sutton,-r.-s.-\(1988\).-learning-to-predict-by-the-methods-of-temporal-differences.-machine-learning,-3-\(1\),-9–44.)) methods to address this challenge by estimating the value of states directly from experience without needing a model of the environment. TD methods, like TD(0), update value estimates based on the difference between predicted and actual rewards received, while Monte Carlo methods estimate value functions by averaging the returns following each visit to a state over many episodes.

#### **Temporal Difference (TD)**

TD methods combine ideas from Monte Carlo methods and Dynamic Programming. They learn directly from raw experience without a model of the environment’s dynamics. For example, in TD(0), the value of a state V (s) is updated using the formula:

V (s) ← V (s) \+ α \[r \+ γV (s′) − V (s)\]

Here, α is the learning rate, r is the reward received, γ is the discount factor, and V (s′) is the value of the next state. This update rule helps adjust the value of the current state towards the observed reward plus the estimated value of the next state.

#### **Monte Carlo (MC)**

MC methods estimate value functions based on the average returns (total accumulated rewards) following each visit to a state. Unlike TD, MC methods require complete episodes to update values, thus working well in episodic tasks. The value of a state V (s) is updated as:

V (s) ← V (s) \+ α \[G*t* − V (s)\]

Where G*t* is the return (sum of discounted rewards) from time t until the end of the episode.

3. ### **On-policy vs Off-policy** {#on-policy-vs-off-policy}

In [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,), the training process and the utilization of policies during training give rise to two primary classes of RL methodologies:

1) **On-policy** Updates the policy while interacting with the environment. This limits exploration as the agent must follow the current policy.

2) **Off-policy** Updates the policy from data collected by another policy. This allows for more efficient exploration as the agent is not restricted by the current policy.

In essence, the choice of approach depends on the complexity of the environment and the desired learning style. Tabular and model-based approaches are good for small, de- terministic environments, while approximate and model-free approaches are better suited for complex, stochastic ones. On-policy learning is efficient for exploitation but lacks exploration, while off-policy learning excels at exploration but can be less efficient for exploitation.

6. ## **Famous Reinforcement Learning Algorithms** {#famous-reinforcement-learning-algorithms}

In the subsequent sections, we highlight three seminal algorithms that have a significant impact on [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) research and applications: Q-learning, [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76)s (DQN), Policy Gradient Methods (REINFORCE), and [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,).

1. ### **Q-learning** {#q-learning}

Q-Learning is one of the oldest and most impactful [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) algorithms in the literature. It’s a tabular off-policy method that aims to learn a mapping from tuples of state-actions to expected cumulative rewards, referred to as Q-values. This algorithm employs a two- dimensional table referred to as the Q-Table, where each row represents a state and each column represents an action. Each entry in the table represents the expected value of the corresponding state and action, which signifies the expected reward that can be earned if that action is taken from that state. Since a table is used to represent all tuples of actions and states, the Q-Learning algorithm is mostly used to solve environments with small state-action spaces, as a large number of states and actions will exponentially increase the size of the Q-Table.

Initially, the Q-Table is filled with initial values that can either be constants like zeros and ones or simply random values. These values are adjusted iteratively during the training until the Q-Table converges to a solution (as shown in Fig. [2.7](#figure-2.7:-solving-a-mdp-using-q-learning)) using the Bellman equation :

Q(s, a) ← Q(s, a) \+ α r \+ γ max Q(s′, a′) − Q(s, a)

*a′*

Where: (s, a) is a state-action tuple, s′ is the next state and a′ is the action maximizing the Q-value from the next state. Bellman equation also has some hyperparameters which are (1) α representing the learning rate that controls the size of the updates in the Q- values, and (2) γ representing the discount factor which controls how much importance to give to the long term reward.

The Bellman equation improves the Q-values of the table by measuring how good each action is in each state. After taking an action and seeing the result, it adjusts the previous estimate of the action’s value by considering the immediate reward r and the potential future rewards from the next state ( max*a′* Q(s′, a′) ). This update is weighted by the learning rate, which balances new information with existing knowledge. Over time, repeatedly applying this process helps refine your strategy, making better decisions to maximize rewards.

Figure 2.7: Solving a MDP using Q-learning

#### **Exploration vs Exploitation**

One crucial aspect of Q-Learning is the choice of actions during training before having a fully trained policy. During training, it tries to strike a balance between exploration (trying new actions to discover their effects) and exploitation (choosing the best-known actions to maximize rewards). This balance is typically managed using an epsilon-greedy strategy, where at each iteration, for a state s, we either choose a random action (explo- ration) with probability ε, or choose the action with the highest Q-Value (exploitation) for that current state s with probability 1 − ε. Usually, we start with a high value of ε at the beginning of training to encourage exploration, and then gradually decrease it to encourage the model to exploit its current knowledge.

2. ### **Deep Q-Network (DQN)** {#deep-q-network-(dqn)}

[DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) (Mnih et al., [2015](#martín-abadi,-ashish-agarwal,-paul-barham,-eugene-brevdo,-zhifeng-chen,-craig-citro,-greg-s.-corrado,-andy-davis,-jeffrey-dean,-matthieu-devin,-sanjay-ghemawat,-ian-goodfellow,-andrew-harp,-geoffrey-irving,-michael-isard,-jia,-y.,-rafal-joze--fowicz,-lukasz-kaiser,-manjunath-kudlur,-.-.-.-xiaoqiang-zheng.-\(2015\).-tensor--flow:-large-scale-machine-learning-on-heterogeneous-systems-[software-available-from-tensorflow.org].-https://www.tensorflow.org/)) is an advanced variant of Q-Learning, where the goal is to replace the Q-Table used in Q-Learning with an approximation function that maps state-action pairs to Q-values. Typically, a neural network is used for this purpose, known as the Q-network. Instead of taking both a state and an action as input to return the Q-value, it takes only the state as input and returns a Q-value for each action from the action space. This [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) method is adopted for complex environments where the search space is too large to be represented using a table due to memory constraints, so we instead learn a mapping between state-action pairs and Q-values.

Similarly to Q-Learning, we use an epsilon-greedy method to balance exploration and exploitation, and we also use the Bellman equation to update our Q-network, with some adjustments.

#### **Target Network**

In Q-Learning, we update the Q-Table one value at a time, keeping the other values fixed to use them in the Bellman equation. This property can’t be easily achieved using a neural network because updating the Q-network for a specific state-action pair will inevitably change the Q-values for all other state-action pairs, as all this information is stored in the weights of the neural network. To maintain consistency in the stored values, we use a second neural network called the target network. This network has the same architecture as the Q-network and periodically loads a copy of the Q-network’s parameters after every K steps. This way, when we update the value for a certain state-action pair, we still have a stable reference for the Q-values of other pairs in the target network.

#### **Experience Replay**

Since updating the Q-network is more complex than updating a Q-table, the authors of [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) proposed using stored data from previous iterations to update the Q-network. This method, referred to as experience replay (Mnih et al., [2015](#martín-abadi,-ashish-agarwal,-paul-barham,-eugene-brevdo,-zhifeng-chen,-craig-citro,-greg-s.-corrado,-andy-davis,-jeffrey-dean,-matthieu-devin,-sanjay-ghemawat,-ian-goodfellow,-andrew-harp,-geoffrey-irving,-michael-isard,-jia,-y.,-rafal-joze--fowicz,-lukasz-kaiser,-manjunath-kudlur,-.-.-.-xiaoqiang-zheng.-\(2015\).-tensor--flow:-large-scale-machine-learning-on-heterogeneous-systems-[software-available-from-tensorflow.org].-https://www.tensorflow.org/)), allows the model to retain knowledge from previous iterations similar to a Q-table. After each iteration, we store the tuple of (state, action, reward, next state) in a buffer, and then sample a batch of tuples from this buffer to update the Q-network.

#### **DQN Algorithm**

The [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) algorithm follows several key steps in its training process. It begins by initial- izing a replay memory D to store the agent’s experiences and a Q-network with random weights θ. For each episode, the algorithm initializes the starting state s and proceeds through a series of time steps. At each time step, the agent selects an action a based on an exploration strategy, executes the action, observes the resulting reward r and new state s′, and stores the transition (s, a, r, s′) in the replay memory. The algorithm then

samples a random mini-batch of transitions from D to update the Q-network’s parameters using gradient descent. Additionally, the target network is updated at regular intervals. This process is repeated across episodes to enable the agent to learn an optimal policy by iteratively refining the Q-values based on accumulated experiences.

Please check Algorithm [1](#algorithm-1-deep-q-network-\(dqn\)) for a pseudo-algorithm of [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76).

**Algorithm 1** Deep Q-Network (DQN)

1: Initialize replay memory D

2: Initialize Q-network with weights θ

3: **for** episode \= 1 to M **do**

4:	Initialize state s

5:	**for** t \= 1 to T **do**

6:	Choose action a using an exploration strategy

7:	Execute action a, observe reward r and new state s′

8:	Store transition (s, a, r, s′) in D  
9:	Sample a random mini-batch of transitions from D 10:	Update Q-network parameters using gradient descent 11:	Update target network every C steps  
12:	Set s ← s′

13:	**end for**

 14: **end for**	

#### **Double DQN**

Double [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76)s (Double DQN) (van Hasselt et al., [2016](#thomas,-m.-\(2004\).-reasoning-about-program-transformations:-imperative-programming-and-flow-of-data-by-jean-francois-collard,-springer-verlag,-2003,-isbn-0-387-95391-4.-journal-of-functional-programming,-14,-589–592.)) is an extension of the original [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) algorithm introduced to address overestimation bias in Q-value estimation. In standard Q-learning, the same values are used both to select and evaluate an action, which can lead to overestimation of action values, particularly in noisy or stochastic environments.

Double [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) tackles the problem of overestimation by separating action selection from value evaluation, employing two distinct networks: an online network for selecting actions and a target network for estimating values. For action selection, the online network iden- tifies the action with the highest Q-value. The target network then assesses the Q-value of the selected action. This method diminishes the bias of overestimation, leading to more precise Q-value estimates and often enhancing learning stability and overall performance.

3. ### **Policy Gradient Algorithm** {#policy-gradient-algorithm}

Unlike value-based methods like Q-Learning, where the goal is to learn a mapping be- tween state-action pairs and values to select the action that maximizes the value, policy gradient approaches (Szepesvári, [2010](#sutton,-r.-s.,-&-barto,-a.-g.-\(2018\).-reinforcement-learning:-an-introduction.-mit-press.-szepesvári,-c.-\(2010\).-algorithms-for-reinforcement-learning.-morgan-claypool-publish-)) aim to find the best parameters θ of the policy π(a|s) that maximize the expected cumulative reward. The expected cumulative reward is mathematically expressed as J(θ) \= E \[ *t* γ*t* · R*t*\], where γ is the discount factor and R*t* is the reward at time step t.

The fundamental concept behind policy gradient methods is to update the policy parameters θ in the direction that increases the likelihood of actions leading to higher rewards. Mathematically, this involves computing the gradient of the expected cumulative reward with respect to the policy parameters (∇*θ*J(θ)).

#### **REINFORCE Algorithm**

One widely used policy gradient method is the REINFORCE algorithm (Williams, [1992](#van-hasselt,-h.,-guez,-a.,-&-silver,-d.-\(2016\).-deep-reinforcement-learning-with-double-q--learning.-proceedings-of-the-thirtieth-aaai-conference-on-artificial-intelligence,-2094–2100.)). The parameters of the trained network are updated using the following rule:

θ ← θ \+ α · ∇*θ* log π(a|s; θ) · G*t*

where α is the learning rate, π(a|s; θ) is the policy, and G*t* is the discounted cumulative return. The gradient ascent step encourages actions that lead to higher rewards while penalizing actions with lower returns.

**Algorithm 2** REINFORCE Algorithm

1: Initialize policy parameters θ

2: **for** episode \= 1 to M **do**

3:	Use policy π*θ* to collect trajectories: s1, a1, r2, . . . , s*T* , a*T* , r*T*  
4:	Compute the return R*t* for each time step  
5:	Compute policy gradient ∇*θ*J(θ) \=	*t* ∇*θ* log π*θ*(a*t*|s*t*) · R*t*  
6:	Update policy parameters using gradient ascent: θ ← θ \+ α∇*θ*J(θ)

 7: **end for**	

A commonly employed variant of the REINFORCE algorithm involves subtracting a baseline value from the return. This adjustment serves to decrease the variance of the gradient estimation while maintaining the bias at its original level. It is advisable to utilize this technique whenever feasible (Sutton & Barto, [2018](#sutton,-r.-s.-\(1988\).-learning-to-predict-by-the-methods-of-temporal-differences.-machine-learning,-3-\(1\),-9–44.)). For example, a common baseline is to subtract state-value from action-value.

4. ### **Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO)** {#proximal-policy-optimization-(ppo)}

[PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) (Schulman et al., [2017](#schulman,-j.,-levine,-s.,-moritz,-p.,-jordan,-m.-i.,-&-abbeel,-p.-\(2015\).-trust-region-policy-optimization.-corr,-abs/1502.05477.-http://arxiv.org/abs/1502.05477)) represents a cutting-edge policy optimization algorithm designed for stability and sample efficiency in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,). Introduced by OpenAI in 2017, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) is categorized as an on-policy algorithm, optimizing the policy while actively collecting new experiences from the environment (Szepesvári, [2010](#sutton,-r.-s.,-&-barto,-a.-g.-\(2018\).-reinforcement-learning:-an-introduction.-mit-press.-szepesvári,-c.-\(2010\).-algorithms-for-reinforcement-learning.-morgan-claypool-publish-)). The core innovation of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) lies in the introduction of a clipped surrogate objective function, ensuring cautious policy updates to maintain stability during training.

One major drawback of policy-based [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) algorithms is their lack of stability, which can lead to quick divergence or convergence to a suboptimal solution. This instability is due to aggressive updates in the policy network that may occur from time to time. Another problem is their complexity and lack of intuitiveness, often involving second- order optimization methods. Additionally, these algorithms often lack a balance between

exploration and exploitation, making it difficult to find a general configuration suitable for various problems, requiring researchers to find specific configurations for each problem instead.

[PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) addresses these problems effectively. It solves the issue of stability by employing a clipped surrogate objective function to limit the change in the policy between updates. This clipping mechanism constrains policy updates, ensuring they do not deviate too much from the current policy, helping maintain stability and preventing destructive updates that can destabilize learning. [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) also combines the clipped surrogate loss with the value function loss and an entropy bonus to encourage exploration, balancing different aspects of learning and contributing to overall stability.

Unlike TRPO (Schulman et al., [2015](#schulman,-j.,-levine,-s.,-moritz,-p.,-jordan,-m.-i.,-&-abbeel,-p.-\(2015\).-trust-region-policy-optimization.-corr,-abs/1502.05477.-http://arxiv.org/abs/1502.05477)), which requires complex second-order optimiza- tion techniques, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) relies on simpler first-order gradient descent methods. This makes [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) easier to implement and more computationally efficient, reducing the overhead as- sociated with optimization.

Additionally, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,)’s formulation with a single, simple objective function and reliance on first-order optimization methods makes it very easy to implement. By avoiding the complexities of TRPO’s trust region optimization, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) has been widely adopted in the RL community. Its straightforward design makes it accessible for those looking to apply RL to various domains without requiring deep expertise in optimization.

#### **Surrogate Clipped Objective**

The objective function of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) is denoted as:

L(θ) \= E\[min(r*t*(θ) · A*t*, clip(r*t*(θ), 1 − ε, 1 \+ ε) · A*t*)\]

where θ is the policy parameters, r*t*(θ) is the likelihood ratio between the new and old policies, A*t* is the advantage function, and ε is a hyperparameter determining the clipping range. This objective function promotes policy updates that do not deviate significantly from the previous policy, preventing large policy changes that could destabilize the learn- ing process. To understand how this works, let’s break down the possible cases for r*t*(θ)  
relative to the clipping threshold (i.e., 1 − ε and 1 \+ ε) and the sign of the advantage Aˆ*t*:

1) **Case 1:** r*t*(θ) within the clipping range 1 − ε ≤ r*t*(θ) ≤ 1 \+ ε. In this case, the clipped objective does not apply, so the surrogate loss is simply:

LCLIP(θ) \= r*t*(θ)Aˆ*t*

.

* **If** Aˆ*t* \> 0 **(advantage is positive)**, this encourages increasing the probability of the action.

  * **If** Aˆ*t* \< 0 **(advantage is negative)**, this encourages decreasing the probability of the action.

2) **Case 2:** r*t*(θ) exceeds the upper clipping threshold r*t*(θ) \> 1 \+ ε. In this case, the ratio is clipped to 1 \+ ε:

LCLIP(θ) \= (1 \+ ε)Aˆ*t*

* **If** Aˆ*t* \> 0 **(advantage is positive)**, the update is limited to avoid too large an increase in the probability of the action.

  * **If** Aˆ*t* \< 0 **(advantage is negative)**, the clipping does not affect the update much, as the policy would already be penalized for increasing the probability of a less advantageous action.

3) **Case 3:** r*t*(θ) falls below the lower clipping threshold r*t*(θ) \< 1 − ε. In this case, the ratio is clipped to 1 − ε

LCLIP(θ) \= (1 − ε)Aˆ*t*

* **If** Aˆ*t* \> 0 **(advantage is positive)**, the clipping does not affect the update

  much, as the policy would already be penalized for decreasing the probability of a more advantageous action.

  * **If** Aˆ*t* \< 0 **(advantage is negative**), the update is limited to avoid too large a decrease in the probability of the action.

In all cases, the model update is designed to prevent aggressive changes in the policy, ensuring more stable learning by clipping the probability ratio r*t*(θ) within the specified range (1 − ε, 1 \+ ε).

The following is the pseudo code of the [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,):

**Algorithm 3** Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO)

1: Initialize policy parameters θ, value function parameters φ

2: **for** iteration \= 1 to N **do**

3:	**for** each environment step **do** 4:		Collect data: (s*t*, a*t*, r*t*, s*t*\+1) 5:	**end for**

6:	**for** epoch \= 1 to K **do**

7:	Compute advantages A*t* using value function

8:	Update policy by maximizing the PPO objective:

9:	ρ ←  *πθ*(*at*|*st*)   
*πθ*old (*at*|*st*)  
10:	θ ← θ \+ α min (ρA*t*, clip(ρ, 1 − ε, 1 \+ ε))  
11:	Update value function by minimizing the mean squared error:  
12:	φ ← φ − β∇*φ*(V*φ*(s*t*) − Vˆ*t*)2  
13:	**end for**  
 14: **end for**	

7. ## **Conclusion** {#conclusion-1}

In this chapter, we talked about [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44), [deep learning (DL)](#dl-deep-learning-29), and focused the most on [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,). [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) is a field of [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) in which an agent interacts with an environment and learns to make

the best decision to achieve a certain goal. The goal of an agent is often formulated as a reward function that should be maximized. [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) is hugely affected by the problem formulation that is constraint by multiple criteria: (1) The complexity of the problem; (2) Whether the transition probabilities are known or not (often not known); (3) Whether we want to train our agent in an online way or an offline way. [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) is a very active field of research, and many algorithms emerged recently and were proven to be very efficient in tackling decision problems, namely: [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76), Policy Gradient Algorithm, and [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,).

# **Chapter 3 Related Work** {#chapter-3-related-work}

In the following section, we will discuss some of the most remarkable works involving the use of [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) ([RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,)) in the task of automatic code optimization. We will decompose each of these works and examine the main components of their [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) systems, including the environment, search space, action space, and more.

1. ## **Halide** {#halide}

Halide (Ragan-Kelley et al., [2013](#pecenin,-m.,-maidl,-a.-m.,-&-weingaertner,-d.-\(2019\).-optimization-of-halide-image-pro--cessing-schedules-with-reinforcement-learning.-anais-do-xx-simpósio-em-sistemas-computacionais-de-alto-desempenho,-37–48.)) is an image-processing compiler and a Domain Specific Language (DSL) dedicated to code optimization. Image processing algorithms in Halide are defined as a multi-stage processing pipeline that performs operations consuming data produced by previous stages and produces results for subsequent stages, until the end of the algorithm. Each stage can be seen as an operation applied on the input data such as blur, convolutions, etc... A pipeline can be represented as a graph connecting different stages, coded using a functional syntax to facilitate understanding and readability of the algorithm (Pecenin et al., [2019](#inc.-http-://-papers-.neurips.cc/-paper-/9015--pytorch--an---imperative---style---high---performance-deep-learning-library.pdf)).

The way memory accesses and the order of computations are managed is not specified at the program level but at the scheduler level. A schedule in Halide can define how a pipeline is compiled into a nested loop by evaluating all necessary computations in each stage.
