To support the creation of a schedule, several researchers have proposed various solu- tions for finding an efficient and optimal pipeline:

#### **Autotuning using Genetic Search (Ragan-Kelley et al., [2013](#pecenin,-m.,-maidl,-a.-m.,-&-weingaertner,-d.-\(2019\).-optimization-of-halide-image-pro--cessing-schedules-with-reinforcement-learning.-anais-do-xx-simpósio-em-sistemas-computacionais-de-alto-desempenho,-37–48.))**

Because the search problem has many local minima, the authors of Halide (Ragan-Kelley et al., [2013](#pecenin,-m.,-maidl,-a.-m.,-&-weingaertner,-d.-\(2019\).-optimization-of-halide-image-pro--cessing-schedules-with-reinforcement-learning.-anais-do-xx-simpósio-em-sistemas-computacionais-de-alto-desempenho,-37–48.)) proposed using a genetic algorithm to find optimal pipelines. However, this approach requires defining specific rules for each program to be optimized. To incorporate prior knowledge about schedules into their mutation rules, the authors select a function at

random and modify its schedule with one of eight randomly selected operations: (1) ran- domize constants, (2) replace a function’s schedule with one randomly generated, (3) copy from a randomly selected function’s schedule, (4) add a transformation to the function’s list of domain transformations, (5) remove a transformation, (6) replace a transformation with one randomly selected, (7) loop fusion, and (8) replace a function’s schedule with one of three common schedule patterns sampled from a text file. However, this approach requires defining specific rules for each optimized schedule, which presents difficulties in overcoming local minima and has a very slow convergence time.

#### **Autotuning using Opentuner (Ansel et al., [2014](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092))**

OpenTuner (Ansel et al., [2014](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)) is a framework designed for the automatic configuration and optimization of software parameters and tuning. It provides a flexible and extensible system to find optimal configurations for various applications, including compilers, [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) algorithms, and software systems. There is also a Halide schedule optimizer based on OpenTuner, which employs more generalized rules. OpenTuner was able to find efficient solutions for simple pipelines (8 stages) but failed to converge for more complex pipelines (32, 44, and 49 stages), with generated schedules being five to ten times slower than manually optimized ones (Ansel et al., [2014](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)).

#### **Automatical scheduling using partitioning (Mullapudi et al., [2016](#mnih,-v.,-kavukcuoglu,-k.,-silver,-d.,-rusu,-a.-a.,-veness,-j.,-bellemare,-m.-g.,-graves,-a.,-riedmiller,-m.,-fidjeland,-a.-k.,-ostrovski,-g.,-et-al.-\(2015\).-human-level-control-through-deep-reinforcement-learning.-nature,-518-\(7540\),-529–533.))**

Another approach extended Halide’s domain-specific analysis mechanism to divide the pipeline stages into groups and find an efficient tile for each group by estimating the arithmetic cost of each stage. The cost is used to define a tile size that minimizes the number of data loads, and the processing loops are ordered to maximize data locality and outer loop parallelism. This approach is easily applicable, with good results, on [Central](#cpu-central-processing-unit-32,-34,-39) [Processing Unit (CPU)](#cpu-central-processing-unit-32,-34,-39) architectures but is not yet supported on [Graphical Processing](#gpu-graphical-processing-unit-32,-34,-41) [Unit (GPU)](#gpu-graphical-processing-unit-32,-34,-41) architectures.

#### **Halide Schedule Optimization with Reinforcement Learning (Pecenin et al., [2019](#inc.-http-://-papers-.neurips.cc/-paper-/9015--pytorch--an---imperative---style---high---performance-deep-learning-library.pdf))**

The authors used [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) ([PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) algorithm) to find an optimal execution schedule for a Halide pipeline on a given hardware platform (Pecenin et al., [2019](#inc.-http-://-papers-.neurips.cc/-paper-/9015--pytorch--an---imperative---style---high---performance-deep-learning-library.pdf)). They minimized a cost function where each scheduling option corresponds to a directive on simple programs. A directive is a simple transformation function like tiling, parallelizing, unrolling, splitting, vectorizing, etc... The learning proceeds in a loop where the pipeline and the scheduler option are input, the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent chooses the optimal execution order by calculating the execution time via compilation and then updates the agent’s parameters. Please examine Fig. [3.1](#figure-3.1:-the-reinforcement-learning-ecosystem-for-halide-\(pecenin-et-al.,-2019\)-the-authors-further-defined-the-components-of-their-rl-system-as-follows:) for an illustration of the overall functioning of [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) in Halide.

Each scheduling option available to the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent corresponds to a single Halide di- rective, including the stage of the pipeline to which the directive should be applied and all the parameters of that directive.  
![][image8]

Figure 3.1: The Reinforcement Learning ecosystem for Halide (Pecenin et al., [2019](#inc.-http-://-papers-.neurips.cc/-paper-/9015--pytorch--an---imperative---style---high---performance-deep-learning-library.pdf)) The authors further defined the components of their [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system as follows:

**The state** The state of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment is represented by the set of directives al- ready used in the scheduling definition. Each stage s, directive d and value a receives a unique numerical identifier, transforming a Halide execution schedule with several direc- tives into a vector of numerical data representing the environment’s state. Please check Fig. [3.2](#figure-3.2:-halide-search-space-represented-as-a-tree-\(pecenin-et-al.,-2019\)) for a representation of the search tree.

Figure 3.2: Halide search space represented as a tree (Pecenin et al., [2019](#inc.-http-://-papers-.neurips.cc/-paper-/9015--pytorch--an---imperative---style---high---performance-deep-learning-library.pdf))

**The action** The actions in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) are represented by the scheduling directives available for a given input pipeline, as specified by the programmer. Each scheduling option ⟨s, d, A⟩ corresponds to a single action, meaning applying a scheduling directive to the input pipeline. Additionally, a special action called no-operation was added, which finalizes the current schedule.

**The reward** The reward is represented by a scalar value returned by the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environ- ment after performing an action. Positive values indicate a reduction in runtime, while negative values indicate a compilation or execution error. The reward r gives more impor- tance to significant reductions in time, but when the execution time increases, the reward is set to zero to avoid penalizing actions that might lead to future gains. The reward is normalized using the initial execution time rtinit of the input pipeline to maintain similar amplitude across different pipelines.

2. ## **Tiramisu** {#tiramisu}

Tiramisu (Baghdadi et al., [2019](#baghdadi,-r.,-merouani,-m.,-leghettas,-m.-h.,-abdous,-k.,-arbaoui,-t.,-benatchba,-k.,-et-al.-\(2021\).-a-deep-learning-based-cost-model-for-automatic-code-optimization.-proceedings-of-machine-learning-and-systems,-3,-181–193.)) is a high-performance compiler designed for optimizing computations on various hardware architectures, including multicore [CPU](#cpu-central-processing-unit-32,-34,-39)s, [GPU](#gpu-graphical-processing-unit-32,-34,-41)s, and specialized hardware accelerators. It is specifically tailored for high-level synthesis of dense and sparse computations, such as those found in linear algebra, deep learning, and image processing. Tiramisu allows developers to write high-level code that specifies the computation, and then it applies a series of transformations to optimize the execution for the target hardware, ensuring efficient parallelization and memory usage.

1. ### **Key features of the Tiramisu** {#key-features-of-the-tiramisu}

Key features of the Tiramisu compiler include:

* **High-Level Abstractions:** Tiramisu provides a high-level programming model that simplifies the expression of complex algorithms while abstracting away the low-level details of hardware-specific optimizations.

  * **Optimization Flexibility:** The compiler supports a wide range of optimization techniques, such as tiling, unrolling, skewing, parallelization and fusion, allowing for fine-tuned performance enhancements.

    * **Target-Independent Code:** Tiramisu separates the computation from the op- timization and hardware-specific details, enabling the same high-level code to be reused across different hardware platforms with minimal modifications.

      * **Automated Scheduling:** It includes a scheduling language that helps define the order and manner in which computations are executed, facilitating automated per- formance tuning.

      * **Integration with [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44):** Recent developments in Tiramisu have explored the in- tegration of [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) techniques, particularly [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,), to automate the optimization and scheduling process, further enhancing its ability to deliver high-performance code.

To automatically find optimal schedules for Tiramisu, an evaluation method needs to be established. The most intuitive way to evaluate a program is to measure its execution

time and then minimize this time or maximize the speedup compared to the initial exe- cution time by finding the most optimal sequence of transformations to apply. However, evaluating Tiramisu programs by executing them can be very costly and time-consuming. Therefore, the authors of Tiramisu aimed to incorporate a cost model based on deep learning (Baghdadi et al., [2021](#bacon,-d.-f.,-graham,-s.-l.,-&-sharp,-o.-j.-\(1993\).-compiler-transformations-for-high--performance-computing.-\(ucb/csd-93-781\).-http-://www2.eecs.berkeley.-edu-/-pubs/techrpts/1993/6309.html)) to estimate program accelerations instead of executing them. This approach aims to speed up the evaluation process of applying transformations to the code. Previously, methods to automatically speed up programs relied on heuristics such as Beam Search and ([Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS)](#mcts-monte-carlo-tree-search-35)).

2. ### **Reinforcement learning in Tiramisu (Hennouni & el Has- sane, [2022](#haykin,-s.-\(1994\).-neural-networks:-a-comprehensive-foundation.-prentice-hall.-hennouni,-n.,-&-el-hassane,-n.-\(2022,-june\).-utilisation-de-l’apprentissage-par-renforce-))** {#reinforcement-learning-in-tiramisu-(hennouni-&-el-has--sane,-2022)}

More recently, an attempt has been made to implement a [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,)\-based solution (Hennouni & el Hassane, [2022](#haykin,-s.-\(1994\).-neural-networks:-a-comprehensive-foundation.-prentice-hall.-hennouni,-n.,-&-el-hassane,-n.-\(2022,-june\).-utilisation-de-l’apprentissage-par-renforce-)). The following represents the main components of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system used in their solution:

#### **Environment**

The decision problem in Tiramisu scheduling can be seen as a [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45) where each state represents the changes in the Tiramisu program representation and action choices. State transitions occur through actions, which correspond to different code transformations to be applied along with their parameters. Before every transition, the Tiramisu auto- scheduler checks if the transformation that is going to be applied is legal. If a code transformation is deemed legal, it allows the transition to a new state with a new obser- vation; otherwise, the system remains in the same state until a legal transformation is selected.

#### **Agent**

The agent is modeled by a simple neural network that receives the observation as input and has two outputs. The first output is the probability distribution over the actions (the policy), and the second is the prediction of the final speedup obtained from that state (the value). The neural network is designed as a feedforward architecture, as shown in Fig. [3.3](#figure-3.3:-architecture-of-the-policy-and-value-network-proposed-in-\(hennouni-&-el-hassane,-2022\).). Once the output is predicted, a mask is applied to the actions to filter out the illegal transformations in that particular state.

#### **Observation**

Since it’s not possible to pass the Tiramisu programs directly to the model, the authors needed to do a feature extraction operation to distinguish between programs and to facil- itate the task of action selection for the policy network. To accomplish this, programs are transformed into a compact vector representation that includes essential characteristics  
![][image9]

Figure 3.3: Architecture of the policy and value network proposed in (Hennouni & el Hassane, [2022](#haykin,-s.-\(1994\).-neural-networks:-a-comprehensive-foundation.-prentice-hall.-hennouni,-n.,-&-el-hassane,-n.-\(2022,-june\).-utilisation-de-l’apprentissage-par-renforce-)).

such as loop information and the actions applied so far. An action mask is also concate- nated to the observation vector, it is a binary vector with a size equal to the number of actions, where each element is either 1 or 0\. A value of 1 at position i indicates that action i is valid, while 0 indicates that the action is invalid, preventing the agent from selecting it. This restriction ensures that the agent can only choose from valid actions. Consequently, after applying an action, its position in the mask becomes 0\.

#### **Action**

The actions available to the agent encompass six transformations: Interchange, Tiling, Skewing, Unrolling, Parallelization, and Reversal. Additionally, an Exit action is included to transition to the terminal state. Initially, the agent selects only the action, and sub- sequently, it defines the parameters associated with the chosen action. This sequential process ensures a structured approach to action selection and parameter definition, facil- itating effective decision-making within the environment.

#### **Reward**

For the reward, two methods were proposed:

1) **Final reward:** This method assigns a reward equal to the final speedup when the episode terminates, indicating the completion of the schedule. While fast, it over- looks intermediate states of the program during the schedule, resulting in less de- tailed feedback regarding which transformations contributed to the overall speedup. Consequently, it provides limited guidance to the policy network.

2) **Immediate reward:** Here, the speedup is computed for each state of the Tiramisu program as it executes, measuring the execution time. Although informative, this method can be slow, especially during early training stages when the policy may

   generate inefficient schedules, potentially resulting in slower programs and hindering the overall training process.

   3. ## **Reinforcement Learning Strategies for High-Level Synthesis** {#reinforcement-learning-strategies-for-high-level-synthesis}

The authors in this work aimed to automate High-Level Synthesis (HLS) code using [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) (Shahzad et al., [2022](#schulman,-j.,-wolski,-f.,-dhariwal,-p.,-radford,-a.,-&-klimov,-o.-\(2017\).-proximal-policy-optimization-algorithms.-proceedings-of-the-34th-international-conference-on-machine-learning-\(icml\),-70,-4651–4660.)), training an agent to select the best passes for more efficient code generation. We refer by "pass" in compilation theory to a transformation that we apply on a code to generate another code. HLS enables developers to implement workloads using high-level language (HLL) codes like C, which can then be compiled into hardware circuits. Typical HLS compilation flows involve several steps:

1) Parsing HLL codes into a generic [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48).

2) Applying an optimizer to the [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48) using various code transformation passes to enhance performance.

3) Mapping optimized [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48) code fragments to hardware blocks.

The paper presents four strategies that utilize [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) for optimizing compiler passes in HLS:

1. ### **Base Learning Strategy** {#base-learning-strategy}

**Environment** The environment is constituted by the LegUp High-Level Synthesis (HLS) tool and the specific application under consideration. This setup generates an output that quantifies the number of clock cycles necessary for executing the designated application. The LegUp HLS tool enables the translation of high-level language descriptions, such as C code, into hardware circuits, facilitating hardware acceleration.

**Agent** Within the context of the given task, the [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) (Schulman et al., [2017](#schulman,-j.,-levine,-s.,-moritz,-p.,-jordan,-m.-i.,-&-abbeel,-p.-\(2015\).-trust-region-policy-optimization.-corr,-abs/1502.05477.-http://arxiv.org/abs/1502.05477)) algorithm is used to train the agent due to its popularity and impact in the literature. The agent is responsible for making decisions on the sequence of optimization passes to be applied during the compilation process.

**Action** Each action corresponds to a distinct optimization pass that can be applied to the code. These actions are systematically appended to an ordered list of actions, forming the episode’s action sequence. This list of actions will later constitute the schedule that is going to be applied in the given code or application.

**State** The state is represented by a histogram of applied passes, known as an action histogram. To implement this histogram, a list equal in length to the number of passes is initialized. Each element of the list is incremented and updated when a pass correspond- ing to the pass number is applied. As shown in Fig. [3.4](#figure-3.4:-the-reinforcement-learning-workflow-in-the-hls-environment-\(shahzad-et-al.,-2022\).) the agent learns to map the distribution of the number of passes applied to the next pass that should be applied to maximize the averaged or expected sum of rewards across time-steps in an episode.

Figure 3.4: The Reinforcement Learning workflow in the HLS environment (Shahzad et al., [2022](#schulman,-j.,-wolski,-f.,-dhariwal,-p.,-radford,-a.,-&-klimov,-o.-\(2017\).-proximal-policy-optimization-algorithms.-proceedings-of-the-34th-international-conference-on-machine-learning-\(icml\),-70,-4651–4660.)).

**Reward** The reward is defined as the difference in cycle count between the previous step of training and the current step. A lower cycle count results in a higher reward value. The reward is set to a default value of 0 for each step, except for the last step in the episode, where the actual clock cycle count is obtained from the HLS tool.

**Episode size** The episode size is fixed at 45 passes. This also imposes an upper bound on the size of the action histogram.

2. ### **Additional Strategies** {#additional-strategies}

**Strategy 1: Pass Ordering** In the typical compiler optimization process, both the frequency and sequence of optimization passes significantly influence the outcome. While the base learning strategy considers pass repetition through an action histogram, pass ordering is only implicit through a list passed to the compiler. This discrepancy between pass repetition and order can lead to different reward values for the same state, potentially making the learning more challenging. To address this, two methods are proposed.

* **Method 1:** involves using the action history instead of the action histogram to explicitly learn pass ordering. Each observation represents the pass applied at a training step.

  * **Method 2:** reward computation frequency is varied while maintaining the action histogram as the observation space. By calculating rewards more frequently, the ordering of multiple sets of passes is factored in, allowing for a more comprehensive learning approach.

**Strategy 2: Action Tuples** Unlike Strategy 1, this strategy emphasizes local pass ordering through the use of action tuples. This approach groups passes that work well together to identify effective pass combinations. The action space is adjusted to represent pass tuples rather than individual passes, enabling the agent to predict multiple passes per step. While larger tuple sizes facilitate the learning of effective pass combinations, overly large tuples may extend learning durations. This mirrors attempting to predict the entire pass ordering in a single step, potentially resulting in larger neural networks and slower learning.

**Strategy 3: Episode Sizing** The episode size plays a significant role in learning quality as it influences the dimensions of the action histogram and the maximum number of passes applied. Smaller episode sizes can enhance learning by reducing the upper bound on the action histogram size, potentially improving efficiency. However, specifying the entire sequence of optimal passes within a limited number of steps may be challenging, restricting room for redundancy or sub-optimal passes. Conversely, larger episode sizes offer more flexibility but may lead to increased combinations of action histograms. Two methods for varying episode sizes are proposed: (1) Method 1 employs static episode sizing, maintaining a fixed size throughout learning, while (2) Method 2 adopts dynamic episode sizing. In the latter approach, the agent learns the optimal pass frequency and the number of passes per episode, with a stop condition introduced to terminate episodes. The aim is for the agent to learn the shortest sequence of passes yielding the highest reward.

**Strategy 4: \-O3 Backend** This strategy leverages the default [CPU](#cpu-central-processing-unit-32,-34,-39) optimization strat- egy (-O3) of a compiler rather than devising a new one from scratch. By utilizing existing knowledge of [CPU](#cpu-central-processing-unit-32,-34,-39) optimization techniques, this strategy may reduce the time and effort required to optimize for HLS.

4. ## **CompilerGym** {#compilergym}

CompilerGym (Cummins et al., [2022](#cooper,-k.-d.,-&-torczon,-l.-\(2011\).-engineering-a-compiler.-elsevier.)) is a library of easy-to-use and performant [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) envi- ronments for compiler tasks. It allows [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) researchers to interact with important compiler optimization problems in a language and vocabulary with which they are comfortable and provides a toolkit for systems developers to expose new compiler tasks for [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) research, aiming to act as a catalyst for making compilers faster using [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44). Key features include:

* **Ease of use:** Built on the popular Gym (Brockman et al., [2016](#brauckmann,-a.,-goens,-a.,-&-castrillon,-j.-\(2021\).-polygym:-polyhedral-optimizations-as-an-environment-for-reinforcement-learning.-2021-30th-international-conference-on-parallel-architectures-and-compilation-techniques-\(pact\),-17–29.-https://-doi.org/10.1109/pact52795.2021.00009)) interface so use Python to write your agent. With CompilerGym, building [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) models for compiler

  research problems is as easy as building [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) models to play video games.

* **Batteries included:** includes everything required to get started. It wraps real- world programs and compilers to provide millions of instances for training and mul- tiple kinds of pre-computed program representations to experiment with end-to-end deep learning, boosted trees, and graph models. It also provides appropriate reward functions and loss functions for optimization.

* **Reproducible:** provides validation for the correctness of results, common baselines, and leaderboards to submit your results.

  Figure 3.5: Workflow of CompilerGym (Cummins et al., [2022](#cooper,-k.-d.,-&-torczon,-l.-\(2011\).-engineering-a-compiler.-elsevier.)). The architecture of CompilerGym is composed of two main components:

* **Frontend:** which is a python library that is built over the famous Gym (Brockman et al., [2016](#brauckmann,-a.,-goens,-a.,-&-castrillon,-j.-\(2021\).-polygym:-polyhedral-optimizations-as-an-environment-for-reinforcement-learning.-2021-30th-international-conference-on-parallel-architectures-and-compilation-techniques-\(pact\),-17–29.-https://-doi.org/10.1109/pact52795.2021.00009)) by OpenAI. It offers a lot of exposure to compilation techniques and optimizations.

* **Backend:** CompilerGym uses a client-server architecture to provide environments for three compiler optimization problems, as depicted in Figure [3.5](#figure-3.5:-workflow-of-compilergym-\(cummins-et-al.,-2022\).-the-architecture-of-compilergym-is-composed-of-two-main-components:). Designed from the outset for large-scale experimentation, these environments are 27 times faster than previous methods, include millions of programs for training, and offer larger search spaces. Additionally, the architecture allows for future extensions using C++, Python, or other client services, supporting both code size and runtime optimization.

  Some of the environments that CompilerGym provides ( As shown in Fig. [3.6](#figure-3.6:-workflow-of-compylergym-\(cummins-et-al.,-2022\)-environments.) ) are:

[**LLVM**](#llvm-low-level-virtual-machine-13,-40,-43,-63) **Phase Ordering** [LLVM](#llvm-low-level-virtual-machine-13,-40,-43,-63) (Lattner & Adve, [2004](#krizhevsky,-a.,-sutskever,-i.,-&-hinton,-g.-e.-\(2012\).-imagenet-classification-with-deep-convolutional-neural-networks.-advances-in-neural-information-processing-sys--tems,-1097–1105.)) is a modular compiler in- frastructure widely used in academia and industry. It analyzes an input source program into a language-agnostic intermediate representation ([IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48)) and then applies a configurable pipeline of optimization passes to the [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48) (Bacon et al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092); Thomas, [2004](#ers.)). The se- lection and order of these compiler optimizations, known as phase ordering, significantly impact the quality of the final binary and have been extensively researched.  
![][image10]

Figure 3.6: Workflow of CompylerGym (Cummins et al., [2022](#cooper,-k.-d.,-&-torczon,-l.-\(2011\).-engineering-a-compiler.-elsevier.)) environments.

**GCC Flag Selection** The [Gnu Compiler Collection (GCC)](#gcc-gnu-compiler-collection-41) flag selection environment exposes the optimization space defined by [GCC](#gcc-gnu-compiler-collection-41)’s command-line flags. This environment works with all versions of [GCC](#gcc-gnu-compiler-collection-41) (Lattner & Adve, [2004](#krizhevsky,-a.,-sutskever,-i.,-&-hinton,-g.-e.-\(2012\).-imagenet-classification-with-deep-convolutional-neural-networks.-advances-in-neural-information-processing-sys--tems,-1097–1105.)). Its main components include:

* **Action:** It provides two interchangeable action spaces. The first directly exposes the optimization space through a list of integers, each encoding the choice of an option with known cardinality. The second action space facilitates [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) tools that operate on a flat list of categorical actions, representing discrete choices the agent can make at each step.

* **Rewards:** Two deterministic reward signals are provided: the byte sizes of the assembly or object code.

* **Observations:** Provides multiple observation spaces that capture relevant infor- mation about the code to adapt it to various methods (deep learning networks, metaheuristics, etc...).

**CUDA Loop Nest Generation** CompilerGym is well-equipped to handle compilers targeting [GPU](#gpu-graphical-processing-unit-32,-34,-41) workloads. It integrates loop\_tool, a simple dense linear algebra compiler that takes a minimalist approach to linear algebra representations by breaking down standard BLAS-type routines into a DAG of n-dimensional applications of arithmetic primitives.

* **Actions:** Multiple transformations including tiling, parallelization, unrolling and skewing that can modify the loop structure are mapped to a discrete action space, meaning each transformation can now be represented with a discrete value for sim- plification.

* **Rewards:** The reward signal is a measure of floating-point operations per second (FLOPs).

* **Observations:** Similarly to GCC Flag Selection, two observation spaces are offered in the setup for better adaptability to different optimization methods.

  5. ## **PolyGym** {#polygym}

Polygym (Brauckmann et al., [2021](#bishop,-c.-m.-\(2006\).-pattern-recognition-and-machine-learning.-springer.)) is an environment for [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) that leverages the poly- hedral model and conforms to the Gym interface (Brockman et al., [2016](#brauckmann,-a.,-goens,-a.,-&-castrillon,-j.-\(2021\).-polygym:-polyhedral-optimizations-as-an-environment-for-reinforcement-learning.-2021-30th-international-conference-on-parallel-architectures-and-compilation-techniques-\(pact\),-17–29.-https://-doi.org/10.1109/pact52795.2021.00009)). This model enables the structured definition of transformations that preserve semantics to improve the performance of a wide range of loops. This method proposes formulating the space of legal transformations within the polyhedral model as a ([MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45)). Instead of using trans- formations, the formulation relies on an abstract space of possible schedules.

However, the main challenge associated with using the polyhedral model as an [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment is the lack of a proper formulation of the search space. High-level loop nest transformations resulting from the polyhedral model, such as loop fusion, tiling, or skewing, are not suitable as actions in an [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment. The first drawback concerns accuracy: not all transformations can be applied to all schedules. This occurs frequently and is computationally expensive. The most significant problem is that the transforma- tions themselves depend on the specific instance of the problem. This work (Brauckmann et al., [2021](#bishop,-c.-m.-\(2006\).-pattern-recognition-and-machine-learning.-springer.)) mainly presents the following contributions:

* It defines the search space of affine loop transformations as an [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45), exposing this problem to new learning algorithms and heuristic models.

* It demonstrates the performance potential of this search space by traversing it using a simple heuristic, then explore the search space of affine loop transformations.

  1. ### **Polyhedral Compilation** {#polyhedral-compilation}

The polyhedral model is a compilation model that uses loop nests to describe program behavior as sets of points representing iterations, forming a lattice polyhedron in a multi- dimensional space. Polyhedral compilers can perform complex restructuring of loop nests and operate at the granularity of loop iterations and instruction instances, enabling the construction of complex loop transformations. In Polygym, a schedule represents trans- formations that preserve semantics by converting affine functions into a set of inequalities.

2. ### **Formulation Using Markov Decision Process ([MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45))** {#formulation-using-markov-decision-process-(mdp)}

The problem of finding an optimal schedule can be divided into two steps. First, the construction of the schedule space and the selection of a schedule. This leads to defining two different MDPs.

**The Construction of the Search Space** The first ([MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45)) represents valid schedules using multidimensional schedules. It defines S*cons* as the set of valid schedules, where each schedule consists of dimensions, instruction dependencies, and other dependencies. Ac- tions can either be (1) increasing dimension (next*dim*); (2) selecting and adding dependen-

cies (select*dep*); (3) increasing the current dependency (next*dep*). Transition probabilities  
P*cons* are determined for each action while always ensuring the validity of schedules.

Figure 3.7: Construction of the search space for the 1st [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45) (Brauckmann et al., [2021](#bishop,-c.-m.-\(2006\).-pattern-recognition-and-machine-learning.-springer.))

**Exploration of the Coefficient Space** After constructing the schedule search space, the schedule’s effectiveness is evaluated by defining it as a point within a polytope, ex- pressed as a convex combination of vertices. This representation enables the introduction of a second ([MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45)) based on the schedule to explore the coefficients’ search space. These coefficients, within the set S*expl*, determine if the schedule is a vertex of the polytope. The [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45) process, depicted in Figure [3.8](#figure-3.8:-exploration-of-the-coefficient-space-in-the-2nd-mdp-\(brauckmann-et-al.,-2021\)), iterates over vertices, selecting coefficients through select*coeff* to define their values.

**Rewards** A feedback loop is defined by the rewards of an [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45). To determine the reward, the two separate MDPs produce a single schedule, which is then compiled with the [LLVM](#llvm-low-level-virtual-machine-13,-40,-43,-63)\-Polly 2 tool and executed. The rewards for both MDPs are defined uniformly, where each [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45) receives a positive reward if the final schedule is valid, a zero reward if the schedule is incomplete, and a negative reward for an invalid schedule.  
![][image11]

Figure 3.8: Exploration of the coefficient space in the 2nd [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45) (Brauckmann et al., [2021](#bishop,-c.-m.-\(2006\).-pattern-recognition-and-machine-learning.-springer.))

6. ## **Synthesis** {#synthesis}

Based on all the related work seen previously, several key insights emerge: (Please refer to Table [3.1](#tiramisu-1) for a summary)

* #### **Advantages of Each Method:**

  * Tiramisu, Halide, and HLS leverage [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) to train agents, enabling them to dis- cover optimal schedules, sequences of passes, or optimizations. This approach harnesses the power of [ML](#ml-machine-learning-15,-17,-19,-22,-29,-32,-34,-39,-40,-44) to automate the optimization process efficiently.

  * PolyGym and CompilerGym provide robust environments for researchers to ex- periment with [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) techniques without encountering technical barriers. These platforms offer versatility and ease of use, facilitating exploration and innova- tion in the field of code optimization.

* #### **Prevalence of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,)\-Based Optimization:**

  * A prevalent trend in the literature is the widespread adoption of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) ([PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,)) as the training algorithm for [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agents in code optimization tasks. This choice highlights the effectiveness and reliability of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) in training agents to make informed decisions and achieve desirable outcomes.

* #### **Evaluation Methods:**

|  | Tiramisu | Halide | HLS | PolyGym | CompilerGym |
| :---- | :---- | :---- | :---- | :---- | :---- |
| Use	poly- hedric model | YES | NO | NO | YES | NO |
| Use	cost model | YES | NO | NO | NO | NO |
| Use in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) so- lution | Agent | Agent | Agent | Environment | Environment |
| Used	algo- rithm | [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) | [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) | [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) | No agent to train | No agent to train |
| Modeling us- ing [MDP](#mdp-markovian-decision-process-1,-35,-42–45) | YES | YES | YES | YES | YES |
| Number	of Actions | 53 | Depend	on the task | Optimization passes | Depend	on the use case | Depend	on the	environ- ment |
| State	repre- sentation | Observation vector	of code features | Halide   di- rectives and their param- eters | Histogram of actions / Or- der of actions | Point in the graph | Intermediate Representa- tion ([IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48)) of the code |
| Reward | Speedup | Speedup | Clock	cycle count | Speedup | Speedup |

Table 3.1: Comparison table with each column taking up 15% of the space

* Notably, different methods employ distinct approaches to evaluate code per- formance. Tiramisu utilizes a cost model to predict acceleration rates, offering insights into potential speedup gains without executing the code.

  * Halide evaluates code performance through code execution, measuring the ex- ecution time to assess optimization effectiveness. This approach provides real- world performance metrics for informed decision-making.

  * HLS relies on clock cycle count as a precise evaluation metric, catering to hardware-specific optimization requirements. By focusing on low-level hard- ware metrics, HLS ensures optimization decisions align with performance tar- gets.

* #### **Diverse State Representations:**

  * Each method adopts its own representation of the current state, tailored to the specific optimization task at hand. Achieving a universal state representation proves challenging due to the diverse nature of optimization tasks.

  * In Tiramisu, authors represent code using a compact vector containing infor- mation about code loops and a history of applied actions. This comprehensive representation facilitates efficient decision-making based on past actions and code structure.

  * Conversely, HLS simplifies state representation by utilizing histograms of ap- plied passes, capturing the sequence of optimizations to inform subsequent decisions. This streamlined approach focuses on the sequence of actions rather than intricate code details.

Despite the diverse approaches and representations employed by each method, they collectively contribute to advancing the field of code optimization through innovative [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,)\-based techniques.

7. ## **Conclusion** {#conclusion-2}

In this chapter, we delved into various studies showcasing the application of [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) in the realm of automatic code optimization. While examining these works, we uncovered com- monalities among them, such as the adoption of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) as a training algorithm and the utilization of speedup as a reward function for optimization purposes. However, we also noted disparities, particularly in the representation of states and actions. Each study employs unique strategies for representing the current state of the optimization problem and determining the actions to take, reflecting the diversity of approaches within this research domain.

This diversity in solutions across the studies proves highly advantageous for the com- pilation research community because it does not only enriches the pool of available ap- proaches but also fosters a deeper understanding of the challenges and opportunities inherent in automatic code optimization. By exploring a range of methodologies and techniques, we gained valuable insights that ultimately guided our exploration, providing rich ideas and methodologies from which to draw inspiration for developing our solution.

# **Chapter 4 Design** {#chapter-4-design}

1. ## **Motivation** {#motivation}

Compilers play a crucial role in translating source code into executable code, but their effectiveness can vary depending on the optimizations they support (Bacon et al., [1993](#ansel,-j.,-kamil,-s.,-veeramachaneni,-k.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-bosboom,-j.,-o’reilly,-u.-m.,-&-amarasinghe,-s.-\(2014\).-opentuner:-an-extensible-framework-for-program-auto--tuning.-2014-23rd-international-conference-on-parallel-architecture-and-compi--lation-techniques-\(pact\),-303–315.-https://doi.org/10.1145/2628071.2628092)). In code optimization, there is a vast search space comprising multiple parameters and configurations that can impact program execution performance. Traditionally, these pa- rameters are manually determined by compilation experts, which can be a lengthy and complex process. To tackle this problem, a lot of research has emerged aiming to find methods to automate this process. Among these methods, [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) presents a promising ap- proach by designing [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agents that autonomously explore large search spaces to identify the best sequence of transformations and their parameters.

The objective of this project is to design a [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system tailored to automatic code optimization for the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler. This involves designing a learning environment to simulate program execution and implementing an intelligent agent capable of making smart decisions in the optimizations to apply. By undertaking this project, we aim to achieve the following objectives:

* **Minimizing program execution times:** The goal of this project is to optimize [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) codes by reducing their execution times.

* **Automation of optimization:** By developing learning agents capable of explor- ing the optimization search space, we seek to automate the compiler optimization process. This will save time and reduce dependence on human experts.

In the following sections, we will begin by modeling the various components of an [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system including actions, states, observations, rewards, policy, agent, and environment, tailored for the project of automatic code optimization in the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler. Initially, the goal is to train an [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system to learn how to optimize individual [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) operations, and then scale up to a more complex setup where we aim to optimize full [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) programs containing multiple operations. Optimizing an operation means finding the best sequence  
![][image12]

Figure 4.1: A set of [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) dialects

of transformations to apply on the operation to minimize its execution time. We refer to such a sequence as the schedule.

[MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) has multiple levels of [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48)s called dialects, each serving a specific purpose, for example, we have the Tensor dialect (Shpeisman et al., [2021](#shivanshu,-a.-\(2023\).-the-ultimate-guide-to-building-your-own-lstm-models.-https://-www.projectpro.io/article/lstm-model/832)) that handles tensor opera- tions, the Linalg dialect (Shpeisman et al., [2021](#shivanshu,-a.-\(2023\).-the-ultimate-guide-to-building-your-own-lstm-models.-https://-www.projectpro.io/article/lstm-model/832)) that handles linear algebra operations, the Affine dialect (Shpeisman et al., [2021](#shivanshu,-a.-\(2023\).-the-ultimate-guide-to-building-your-own-lstm-models.-https://-www.projectpro.io/article/lstm-model/832)) to represent operations at the level of affine loops, and the Transform dialect to apply code transformations on the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code. [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) has also the ability to lower from one [IR](#ir-intermediate-representation-13,-14,-37,-40,-45,-48) to another one, for instance, we can go from the Tensor dialect to the Linalg dialect, and from the Linalg dialect to the Affine dialect. In the context of our solution, we choose to optimize operations at the Linalg dialect level for the following reasons:

* It already has many transformations (tiling, interchange and vectorization).

* Multiple deep learning frameworks can be lowered into the Linalg dialect, including Tensorflow (Martín Abadi et al., [2015](#lattner,-c.,-amini,-m.,-bondhugula,-u.,-cohen,-a.,-davis,-a.,-pienaar,-j.,-riddle,-r.,-shpeisman,-t.,-vasilache,-n.,-&-zinenko,-o.-\(2021\).-mlir:-scaling-compiler-infras--tructure-for-domain-specific-computation.-2021-ieee/acm-international-sympo--sium-on-code-generation-and-optimization-\(cgo\),-2–14.-https://-doi-.org/10.-1109/cgo51591.2021.9370308)) and Pytorch (Paszke et al., [2019](#neptune.ai.-\(2024\).-neptune.ai:-experiment-tracker.-https://neptune.ai)).

* No need for a legality check. Generally, after applying transformations to a code, its behavior can be altered. However, the way the Linalg dialect is built guarantees that the applied transformations don’t alter the behavior of the code, keeping it correct. This saves us a lot of time that would otherwise be spent on legality checks.

  2. ## **Actions** {#actions}

An action allows the agent to transition from state S*t* to state S*t*\+1. In the context of this project, an action represents either the optimization to be applied to one or more loops

of an operation, or finishing the schedule of the current operation and moving to the next one. In our method, we focus on the following transformation functions to optimize [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code: [Tiling (T)](#t-tiling-49,-50,-53,-68), [Tiling \+ Parallelization (TP)](#tp-tiling-+-parallelization-49,-50,-53,-68), Interchange, and Vectorization. We also eventually include Fusion for a second set of experiments where we optimize full [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) programs by attempting to fuse multiple operations together.

An action should be characterized not only by the transformation to apply but also by its parameters and how it is applied to the operation. For instance, an action using Tiling should be characterized by the tiling size to use and which loops to apply the tiling to. Assuming our operation has N loops and M possible tiling sizes, we have the following action space sizes for each transformation:

* **Tiling action space size:** M *N* , because there are M possible tiling sizes for each of the total N loops.

* **Parallelization+Tiling action space size:** the same as Tiling, M *N* .

* **Interchange action space size:** N\!, which is equal to the number of possible permutations of N numbers.

* **Vectorization action space size:** 1, because it’s a binary action: either we vec- torize or we do not.

As illustrated above, the action space can exponentially expand, making the task of identifying optimal optimizations impractical. For instance, if a schedule can contain up to 5 optimizations, the space of schedules expands to:

(M *N* \+ M *N* \+ N\! \+ 1\)5

Considering N \= 7 loops per operation and only M \= 5 possible tile sizes, the total action space would amount to 8.63 x 1022. To address this, an intuitive solution is to limit the number of possible loops (N) and the number of possible tiling sizes (M):

* For the number of possible loops N, reducing it may not be wise as many computa- tionally expensive operations, like 2D convolutions with 7 loops and 3D convolutions with 10 loops, have a high number of loops. Ignoring these loops means neglecting the optimization of operations known to be computationally intensive and significant in scientific code.

* For the number of possible tiling sizes M, reducing it is tolerable, although it may result in missing optimal optimizations by not having the most optimal tile size in the search space.

To tackle this problem, we developed two representations for the action space and experimented with both. We call them: 1\) Unrolled Action Space and 2\) Hierarchical Action Space.

1. ### **Unrolled Action Space** {#unrolled-action-space}

In this representation, we include all possible combinations of actions, spanning all trans- formations and their respective parameters. This results in a large action space. In this method, at each timestep, we pick one action from the list of all possible actions. This picked action contains all information about the transformation and its parameters to apply to the operation. An example is shown in Fig. [4.2](#figure-4.2:-one-action-from-the-unrolled-action-space.).

Figure 4.2: One action from the Unrolled Action Space.

2. ### **Hierarchical Action Space** {#hierarchical-action-space}

In this representation, we aim to represent the action space as the Cartesian product of actions, with each action represented by a tuple of subactions. In this method, at each timestep, we pick multiple actions to construct the final action. Practically, we first pick the transformation to apply (from a small list containing [T](#t-tiling-49,-50,-53,-68), [TP](#tp-tiling-+-parallelization-49,-50,-53,-68), Interchange, and Vectorization), and then we pick their respective parameters. Even with this partitioning, we expect the number of possible hyperparameters to be vast. To counter this, we further partition the space of possible hyperparameters for each transformation as shown in Fig. [4.3](#figure-4.3:-one-action-from-the-hierarchical-action-space.):

* [**TP**](#tp-tiling-+-parallelization-49,-50,-53,-68) **and [T](#t-tiling-49,-50,-53,-68):** Since [TP](#tp-tiling-+-parallelization-49,-50,-53,-68) and [T](#t-tiling-49,-50,-53,-68) are applied at the loop level of the operation, each loop should either have a tiling size unless we don’t tile it. Assuming we have N loops, we pick N tile sizes, one for each loop. We represent the space of all possible tile sizes as the Cartesian product of the possible tile sizes for each loop, which is M x M x . . . x M (N times).

  * **Interchange:** It’s harder to partition the space of all possible interchange combi- nations because it’s a transformation affecting a whole loop nest, involving at least two loops to be permuted. For simplicity, we keep a list of all possible permutations of two consecutive loops as we can construct any permutation of N loops using a composition of permutations of two consecutive loops.

    * **Vectorization:** This is simple as it doesn’t have any parameters; it vectorizes the entire operation.

      ![][image13]

Figure 4.3: One action from the Hierarchical Action Space.

3. ## **States and Observations** {#states-and-observations}

Given that passing code operations as raw textual data to the model isn’t very practical, we need to conduct feature extraction to represent each operation with key features before providing it as input to the model. In this section, we detail on the selection of features extracted from the operation, which will later be concatenated to form a representation of the operation.

* **The loops information:** The idea is to get the main characteristics of a loop, which are the lower bound, the upperbound, and the increment for each loop from the Linalg operation. We do achieve this by lowering the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) operation from the Linalg dialect to the Affine dialect in which the loops are explicitly expressed. After analyzing all Linalg operations, we noticed that they all have a lower bound of 0 and an increment of 1, so since they are both constant, we chose to discard this information and only keep the upper bound information. This will help us maintain a smaller representation size containing only relevant information.

* **Load access matrices:** Each Linalg operation can be represented by a loop nest. In the loop nest, data is loaded from one or multiple arrays, calculations are per- formed, and the result is stored in an output array. To load the data, we need to access it using indices based on the loop iterators. Therefore, it is useful to have information about which loop iterators were used and how they were used to access data from the input arrays. Inspired by (Baghdadi et al., [2021](#bacon,-d.-f.,-graham,-s.-l.,-&-sharp,-o.-j.-\(1993\).-compiler-transformations-for-high--performance-computing.-\(ucb/csd-93-781\).-http-://www2.eecs.berkeley.-edu-/-pubs/techrpts/1993/6309.html)), we represent the indexing of each input array using an access matrix. The access matrix has D rows and N \+ 1 columns, where D is the number of dimensions of the input array and N is the number of loops. Each row in the matrix corresponds to a dimension of the array, and each column corresponds to a loop iterator. The array dimensions are expressed as linear combinations of the loop iterators. The coefficients of these loop iterators are stored in their respective columns. The last column in the matrix represents constants. Note that we can have multiple array loads in a single loop nest of a Linalg operation. For instance, we have two array loads in matrix multi- plication since we need to get the value from the first matrix and the value from the second matrix.

* **Store access matrices:** Similarly to load access matrices, we also store the cal- culated result into an output array using indices based on the loop iterators. We

  ![][image14]

Figure 4.4: Example of an access matrix.

represent this using the same formulation. Note that Linalg operations have at most one output array, and thus we can have at most one store access matrix.

* **Mathematical operations count:** We count the number of each mathematical operation, including arithmetic operations like addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division, as well as mathematical functions like exponential and logarithmic functions used in softmax and loss functions like cross-entropy.

* **History of optimizations:** Since each optimization changes the operation, we need to track the history of all transformations applied to it. To achieve this, we can simply track the history of optimizations applied on each loop.

All these features are then combined to form an observation vector. To fix the size of this vector, we need to make the following assumptions:

* **Maximum number of loops** N **:** If an operation has fewer loops, we add padding. We set this maximum number of loops to 10 to cover all Linalg operations.

* **Maximum number of access matrices per operation** L**:** After examining all Linalg operations, we determined that the maximum number of array loads in a single operation is 6\. If an operation has fewer access matrices, we fill the rest with padding.

* **Maximum number of indices in the access matrices** D**:** After examining all Linalg operations, we determined that the maximum number of indices in array loads and stores is 4\. If an access matrix has fewer indices, we fill the rest with padding.

* **Maximum number of steps in each episode MAX\_STEPS:** In our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) envi- ronment, this number represents the maximum number of transformations that can be applied to an operation.

  By applying the above assumptions, we can infer the size of each feature:

* **Loop Information shape:** N; we take one value for each loop from the N loops which is the upper bound.

* **Load Access Matrices:** L x D x (N \+ 1\); because we have L matrices of shape  
  D x (N \+ 1\).

* **Store Access Matrix shape:** D x (N \+ 1\); because we have only one store matrix of shape D x (N \+ 1\).

* **Mathematical Operations Count shape:** 6; we count the operations: addition, subtraction, multiplication, division, exponential, and logarithmic.

* **History of Optimizations shape:** N x 3 x MAX\_STEPS; because each loop n ∈ {1, . . . , N} can have 3 transformations applied: [TP](#tp-tiling-+-parallelization-49,-50,-53,-68), [T](#t-tiling-49,-50,-53,-68), and Interchange. Each transformation can be applied at any step of the episode. Therefore, History\[n, t, s\] represents the parameter of transformation t applied on loop n at step s. For [T](#t-tiling-49,-50,-53,-68) and [TP](#tp-tiling-+-parallelization-49,-50,-53,-68), History\[n, t, s\] represents the tiling size. For Interchange, History\[n, t, s\] represents the index of the permutation used.

  So in total, we end up with an observation vector of size:

N \+ (L x D x (N \+ 1)) \+ (D x (N \+ 1)) \+ 6 \+ (N x 3 x MAX\_STEPS)

where:

* N is the maximum number of loops.

* L is the maximum number of load access matrices per operation.

* D is the maximum number of indices in the access matrices.

* MAX\_STEPS is the maximum number of steps in each episode.

  4. ## **Rewards** {#rewards}

In the context of [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,), reward is a quantitative measure used to evaluate the performance of an agent in a given environment. This reward is typically defined by the system user and represents the goal to be achieved by the agent. The objective of learning is to find the optimal policy that will allow the agent to maximize the sum of rewards obtained during the experience.

In the case of a compiler, it’s intuitive to think of the acceleration rate of the final code over the initial code as a reward that we want to maximize. Two main methods come to mind:

1) **Calculate the Acceleration After Each Optimization:** This involves calculat- ing the acceleration after each optimization and then computing the overall acceler- ation rate by multiplying the acceleration rates of each action. This method ensures that the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent gets accurate feedback for each optimization applied. However, it can be very time-consuming because calculating the acceleration requires executing the code to measure the execution time, which is especially demanding for computa- tionally expensive operations such as 2D and 3D convolutions. We call this method **"Immediate reward"**.

2) **Calculate the Reward Once at the End of All Optimizations:** This method involves calculating the reward only once, at the end of all optimizations (i.e., at the end of the schedule). It is much faster as it eliminates the need to execute the code multiple times for each applied optimization. Instead, the code is executed only once at the end. Practically, we assign a reward to all optimizations except for the last one, which is the final acceleration over the initial code. We call this method **"Final reward"**.

However, in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,), we aim to maximize the sum of rewards rather than the product of rewards, necessitating reward preprocessing before using them. Specifically, we prefer to take the logarithm of the reward, because the log converts multiplications into additions. This approach reduces variance, creates smoother rewards, and is robust against outlier values by squeezing values into a smaller range, avoiding excessively large rewards. Addi- tionally, the log transformation naturally converts values between 0 and 1 into negative values, and values greater than 1 into positive values. As a result, poor acceleration (be- tween 0 and 1\) results in a negative reward, and good acceleration (greater than 1\) results in a positive reward.

5. ## **Policy** {#policy}

In [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) , the policy is a crucial concept that defines the behavior of an agent in an en- vironment. It is a mapping from the states of the environment to the actions that the agent should take when in those states. More formally, it is a function π that maps each state s ∈ S to a probability distribution over actions. Specifically, π(a|s) represents the probability of taking action a when in state s. Here, π(a|s) \= P(A*t* \= a | S*t* \= s), where A*t* is the action taken at time t, and S*t* is the state at time t. Thus, the policy specifies a probability distribution over possible actions for each state.

The policy is central to the decision-making process of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent. It guides the agent on how to act at each step based on the current state, to maximize cumulative rewards over time. The objective of [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) algorithms is to find an optimal policy, π∗, that maximizes the expected return (cumulative reward) from any initial state. For a given policy π, the value function V *π*(s) represents the expected return when starting in state s and following policy π thereafter.

In deep [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,), we can represent such a policy with a trainable neural network whose goal is to converge to the optimal policy. In the context of our solution, we use a feedforward neural network that takes the observation of the current code as input and outputs the expected value and a probability distribution over the set of actions to take. Practically, our neural network architecture is composed of two parts, the first part is the backbone which takes the representation vector as input, processes it, and then passes it to the policy network and the value network:

* **Backbone:** This is the beginning of our network, which receives the observation vector as input and processes it. It consists of three fully connected layers of 1024

  nodes with [Rectified Linear Unit (ReLU)](#relu-rectified-linear-unit-55,-87,-91) activation in between. The [ReLU](#relu-rectified-linear-unit-55,-87,-91) activa- tion introduces non-linearity, enabling the model to learn more complex representa- tions. The output of the backbone is then passed in parallel to two other networks: the value network and the policy network.

* **Value Network:** The purpose of this network is to take the processed represen- tation of the observation vector and return the expected return, or reward. This part is very beneficial for [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) as it provides an estimate of the potential reward starting from this state, helping to normalize the rewards and guide the policy. In [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,), this part is called the Critic. It consists of two fully connected layers with 128 and 1 node, respectively, with [ReLU](#relu-rectified-linear-unit-55,-87,-91) activation in between.

* **Policy Network:** The goal of the policy network is to take the processed repre- sentation of the observation vector and map it into a distribution over the action space. This part predicts the action to take based on the current state of the code encoded in the observation vector. Since the policy network is responsible to return the actions, its architecture is highly dependent on the representation of the action space as shown previously: Unrolled Action Space or Hierarchical Action Space.

#### **Policy Network with Unrolled Action Space**

| Interchange (1, 0, 2\) |
| :---- |
| **Interchange** (0, 2, 1\) |
| **Interchange** (2, 1, 0\) |

| Tiling (0, 0, 0\) |
| :---- |
| **Tiling** (0, 0, 2\) |
| … |
| **Tiling** (0, 0, 256\) |
| **Tiling** (0, 2, 0\) |
| **Tiling** (0, 2, 2\) |
| … |
| **Tiling** (256, 256, 128\) |
| **Tiling** (256, 256, 256\) |

| TP (0, 0, 0\) |
| :---- |
| **TP** (0, 0, 2\) |
| … |
| **TP** (0, 0, 256\) |
| **TP** (0, 2, 0\) |
| **TP** (0, 2, 2\) |
| … |
| **TP** (256, 256, 128\) |
| **TP** (256, 256, 256\) |

MLIR code representation

Figure 4.5: A diagram of the policy network using the Unrolled Action Space.

As shown in Fig. [4.5](#interchange-\(1,-0,-2\)), the implementation is straightforward in this case. Since we explicitly have all the combinations of possible actions, a simple fully connected layer with several nodes equal to the size of the action space will suffice. We then sample one action from this set of actions, typically the action with the highest probability.

#### **Policy Network with Hierarchical Action Space**

| 0 | 2 | 8 | 32 | 128 | 256 |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---- | :---- | :---- |

| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---- | :---- | :---- |

**Pick 3 actions**

| 0 | 2 | 8 | 32 | 128 | 256 |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---- | :---- | :---- |

|  Parallelization Network | *Loop 1* |
| ----- | :---: |
|  | *Loop 2* |
|  | *Loop 2* |

**Pick 3 actions**

| 0 | 2 | 8 | 32 | 128 | 256 |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---- | :---- | :---- |

| 0 | 2 | 8 | 32 | 128 | 256 |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---- | :---- | :---- |

| 0 | 2 | 8 | 32 | 128 | 256 |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---- | :---- | :---- |

Figure 4.6: A diagram of the policy network using the Hierarchical Action Space.

As shown in Fig. [4.6](#mlir-code-representation), the implementation is more complicated because the action space is partitioned into smaller action spaces, requiring us to sample more than one action to construct the final action. First, we need to select which transformation to apply from Tiling , Tiling+Parallelization , Interchange, and Vectorization. This is done using a fully connected network with 4 nodes (one for each possible transformation). After selecting the transformation, we need to select its parameters. To achieve this, we implement a branch of the neural network for each transformation to output the parameters of the transformations. Specifically, we have:

* **Tiling and Tiling+Parallelization:** We need to determine which loops will be tiled and with what tile sizes. For simplicity, we assume that a tile size of 0 means the loop will not be tiled, the each loop has (M \+ 1\) possible tile sizes: one tile size equal to zero for no tiling and M tile sizes greater than zero indicating real tile sizes. To achieve this, we use two fully connected layers: the first with 512 nodes and the second (the output of the policy network) with N x (M \+ 1\) nodes. This is because we need to find the tile size for each of the N loops, and each loop has (M \+ 1\) possible tile sizes. For simplicity, we reshape the output layer from N x (M \+ 1\) to (N, M \+ 1\), then we take the distribution over the (M \+ 1\) tile sizes for each of the N loops and select the tile size for each loop. This gives us N tile sizes, one for each loop, thereby providing the parameters for Tiling (or Tiling+Parallelization). Check Fig. [4.7](#t10).

* **Interchange:** Interchange is simpler because it is not a loop-wise transformation but rather a nested loop transformation, meaning we do not need a hyperparameter for each loop but instead one hyperparameter for the entire loop nest. Since we

  decided to only allow permutations of two consecutive loops, we have N possible actions (including permuting the first and last loops). We use two fully connected layers: the first with 512 nodes and the second (the output of the policy network) with N nodes. We take the distribution over these N nodes and sample the action, which represents which permutation to apply, thus providing the parameter for the Interchange transformation. Check Fig. [4.8](#interchange-1).

* **Vectorization:** This is the simplest case because Vectorization does not have any parameters and thus does not have a corresponding branch in the policy network.

| T10 | T11 | … | T1M | T12 |
| ----- | ----- | :---: | ----- | ----- |
|  T20 |  T21 | … |  T2M |  T25 |
| … | … | … | … | … |
|  TN0 |  TN1 | … |  TNM |  TN1 |
