6. ## **Agent** {#agent}

In our main experiments, we chose to use [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) (Schulman et al., [2017](#schulman,-j.,-levine,-s.,-moritz,-p.,-jordan,-m.-i.,-&-abbeel,-p.-\(2015\).-trust-region-policy-optimization.-corr,-abs/1502.05477.-http://arxiv.org/abs/1502.05477)) to train our agent. We chose this algorithm due to its significant impact in the literature and its widespread use in many research projects (Baghdadi et al., [2021](#bacon,-d.-f.,-graham,-s.-l.,-&-sharp,-o.-j.-\(1993\).-compiler-transformations-for-high--performance-computing.-\(ucb/csd-93-781\).-http-://www2.eecs.berkeley.-edu-/-pubs/techrpts/1993/6309.html); Ragan-Kelley et al., [2013](#pecenin,-m.,-maidl,-a.-m.,-&-weingaertner,-d.-\(2019\).-optimization-of-halide-image-pro--cessing-schedules-with-reinforcement-learning.-anais-do-xx-simpósio-em-sistemas-computacionais-de-alto-desempenho,-37–48.); Shahzad et al., [2022](#schulman,-j.,-wolski,-f.,-dhariwal,-p.,-radford,-a.,-&-klimov,-o.-\(2017\).-proximal-policy-optimization-algorithms.-proceedings-of-the-34th-international-conference-on-machine-learning-\(icml\),-70,-4651–4660.)) involving [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) for code optimization similar to our project. Some of the advantages of using [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) are:

* **Stability and Reliability:** [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,)’s surrogate objective function prevents large pol- icy updates, which is crucial for stability in learning. In the context of compiler optimizations, where the effects of actions can vary greatly, maintaining stability ensures that the learning process remains consistent and reliable. This stability allows the agent to learn and adapt gradually, without experiencing drastic fluctu- ations in performance.

* **Clipped Probability Ratios:** [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,)’s use of clipped probability ratios helps pre- vent large deviations in policy updates. By limiting the size of these updates, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) ensures that the policy changes smoothly, avoiding abrupt shifts that could disrupt the learning process. This technique enhances the stability of training, making it easier for the agent to converge to an optimal policy.

* **Effective Handling of High-Dimensional Action Spaces:** Compiler optimiza- tion tasks often involve complex action spaces with multiple decisions to be made in sequence. [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) is well-suited for managing such high-dimensional action spaces, as it can effectively explore and exploit the vast array of possible actions. Its ability to handle complex action spaces allows [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) to learn intricate strategies for compiler optimizations, leading to improved performance of compiled code.

* **Robustness to Hyperparameter Variations:** [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) exhibits robustness to vari- ations in hyperparameters, reducing the need for extensive tuning. This robustness means that [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) can perform effectively across a range of settings without requiring meticulous parameter adjustments. As a result, developers can focus more on de- signing effective reward structures and observation spaces, rather than fine-tuning hyperparameters, streamlining the development process.

  7. ## **Environment** {#environment}

One of the main components in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) is the environment, which is the system from which the agent receives observations of the state and to which it sends actions to be executed. In the context of our solution, we spent a lot of time designing the environment, optimizing it, and adapting it to our different methods.

An [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment usually has two main functions: the reset function and the step function. The reset function is responsible for initializing the environment and resetting it for a new episode, whereas the step function is responsible for taking an action as input, executing it on the current state of the environment, and returning the next state, a reward, and other relevant information.

It’s worth noting that during a training episode, we keep track of various types of information, such as the current optimized [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code, the current execution time, the current reward, the history of taken actions, and the number of steps taken. We use this information to make decisions in the environment, specifically to decide which actions should be masked for future steps which actions are allowed, and when to stop the episode and start a new one.

In the following, we will delve deeper into each of these functions:

1. ### **Reset Function** {#reset-function}

This function is responsible for resetting the environment and preparing it for a new episode. In this function, we randomly select a Linalg operation that we want to optimize and reset all stored information from the last episode since it’s a new episode.

2. ### **Step Function** {#step-function}

This function takes as input the action picked by the agent and applies it to the current state of the environment, returning the next state and a reward that serves as feedback for the agent. It also updates the stored information of the environment. Since this step function handles all code transformations, reward calculations, and decisions about future steps are done here, it’s crucial to optimize it as it represents a significant portion of the computational time of the whole [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system. To achieve fast performance, we performed many experiments with multiple configurations and even built some schedules manually to understand what results in a good schedule for the environment. After extensive analysis, here are our deductions:

1) It’s always better to perform Tiling+Parallelization only once on the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code, as multiple applications of this transformation consistently result in worse performance. Therefore, once this action is taken, we will mask it in future steps so that it doesn’t get picked again by the agent.

2) It’s crucial to perform Tiling+Parallelization or Tiling transformations on the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code before vectorizing the Linalg operations. If we don’t tile the operation be- fore vectorizing, it generates a large amount of machine code (gigabytes of code), which leads to system collapse due to memory limitations and compilation errors. Hence, the vectorization action remains masked until the agent picks a Tiling or Tiling+Parallelization action, after which it will be unmasked, and the agent is allowed to select it.

3) In the case of convolution operations, we noticed that they cannot be vectorized because this feature is not yet implemented in the compiler. Instead, they should first be decomposed into 1D convolutions and then vectorized normally. Thus, whenever the agent picks the vectorization action for convolution, we first decompose it into 1D convolutions and then vectorize. The same approach is applied to max- pooling.

4) We also noticed that some bad schedules result in very slow code, which can sig- nificantly slow down the training process. To avoid this, we set a time threshold to interrupt executions that take more than one minute and return a very negative reward so that the agent learns to avoid such schedules.

By taking all these considerations into account, we managed to develop a fast environ- ment that allowed us to perform many experiments with multiple configurations. More details about the environment are provided in the Implementation chapter.

8. ## **Fusion and Computational Graphs** {#fusion-and-computational-graphs}

One of the main milestones of this project is to optimize scientific codes comprising of multiple operations like neural networks and not just one operation. To achieve such a thing, a very useful transformation needs to be added to the agent and the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment which is Fusion.

Fusion is a widely used optimization technique in compilers that combines two opera- tions forming a producer-consumer relationship into a single operation. This is achieved by identifying and merging the loops they share, typically the outermost loop, to improve performance and data locality. The main goal of fusion is to ensure data is used close to where it is produced, thereby reducing cache misses.

In our implementation, we first parse the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code to construct a [Directed Acyclic](#dag-directed-acyclic-graph-60) [Graph (DAG)](#dag-directed-acyclic-graph-60). The nodes in this graph represent the operations in the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code, and the edges represent the producer-consumer relationships between these operations. Two connected nodes can be fused. We build this graph using the C++ [API](#api-application-programming-interface-60,-63,-70) of [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) by traversing the entire code. For each operation, we examine its input operands and trace back to the operations that produced them. This process is repeated iteratively until the entire computational graph is explored. The structured graph is then sent to Python for use in the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment.

1. ### **Update to the Environment** {#update-to-the-environment}

In our solution, we always fuse the current operation with its closest producer. This means that it starts with its immediate producers and then progresses to the producers of these producers, and so on.

The steps of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment with Fusion included can be summarized as follows:

1) We select a producer operation to potentially fuse with some of its consumers.

2) We optimize this producer operation by finding the best sequence of transformations to apply (as demonstrated in the previous chapters).

3) After optimizing the producer, we examine its producers to determine which oper- ations to fuse. We may encounter scenarios where we fuse no consumers or fuse all consumers.

4) After selecting the operations to fuse, we fuse them with the producer, and then proceed to optimize them individually.

5) After optimizing all fused operations, we move to another producer and repeat the process until no more potential consumer is found in the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code.

   2. ### **Update to the Observation** {#update-to-the-observation}

In the previous setup, we used the features of one operation in an observation vector for the agent. Since Fusion involves two operations, we need to adjust the observation vector:

* **Consumer and Producer Observations:** Include the observation vector of the consumer (current operation) and, if available, the observation vector of the imme- diate producer. If there is no producer, we use a padding of zeros. These vectors are concatenated to form a single observation vector containing information about both the consumer and the producer.

  * **Action History:** We do not include the action history of the producer in its observation vector, as it hasn’t been optimized yet. We will enforce the same tiling size used on the consumer to enable fusion.

    3. ### **Update to the Agent** {#update-to-the-agent}

The agent now processes a larger input since the observation vector includes information about both the consumer and the producer. This expanded observation vector provides the agent with a more comprehensive understanding of the relationship between the op- erations, allowing it to make more informed decisions about optimization strategies.

Additionally, the agent has a new action to support: Fusion. This action can be implemented using a binary prediction node within the agent’s policy network. The node outputs a 1 to indicate that fusion should be applied and a 0 to indicate that fusion should not be applied. We can simply achieve this by using a Sigmoid activation function or a simple Binary activation function (Dubey et al., [2021](#cummins,-c.,-wasti,-b.,-guo,-j.,-cui,-b.,-ansel,-j.,-gomez,-s.,-jain,-s.,-liu,-j.,-teytaud,-o.,-steiner,-b.,-et-al.-\(2022\).-compilergym:-robust,-performant-compiler-optimiza--tion-environments-for-ai-research.-2022-ieee/acm-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization-\(cgo\),-92–105.)).

9. ## **Conclusion** {#conclusion-3}

In this chapter, we thoroughly explored the design process for each aspect of the agent. We began by addressing how to handle states and then progressed to defining actions, rewards, and the overarching policy architecture. At each step, we explained the reasoning behind our chosen methodologies. Specifically, we introduced two formulations for actions: the Unrolled Action Space and the Hierarchical Action Space, delving into the specifics of each. Similarly, we presented two methods for rewards: either calculating acceleration after each optimization or computing it once after all optimizations, highlighting the pros and cons of each approach. Within the policy framework, we detailed the design of the model architecture and its adaptability to different action space representations. Lastly, we discussed why [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) was chosen to address the challenges of automatic code optimization within the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) framework, and underlined some deductions that we obtained from analyzing [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) operations and schedules.

# **Chapter 5 Implementation** {#chapter-5-implementation}

After discussing the design of our solution, in this chapter we delve into the technical details, highlighting the tools and frameworks used to support our work. We explain how we established a connection between our work in Python and the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler, and provide further details about the environment and training algorithms. Finally, we discuss the fusion transformation and the updates required to adapt our method to such a complex transformation.

1. ## **Tools** {#tools}

To conduct comprehensive experimentation and effectively manage the exploration of multiple configurations within our solution, we collected a suite of tools that were useful in facilitating the implementation of our environment and neural networks, visualizing experiment logs, and facilitating result comparison. The tools utilized in our research include:

#### **Torch**

Torch (Paszke et al., [2019](#neptune.ai.-\(2024\).-neptune.ai:-experiment-tracker.-https://neptune.ai)), more commonly known as PyTorch, is an open-source machine learning library developed by Facebook’s AI Research Lab (FAIR). It is widely used for deep learning applications due to its dynamic computation graph and easy-to-use interface. PyTorch supports tensor computations with strong GPU acceleration, making it ideal for developing and training neural network models. Its flexibility and support for dynamic neural networks are particularly beneficial in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,), where the structure of the computation graph can change frequently.

#### **Neptune**

Neptune (neptune.ai, [2024](#mullapudi,-r.-t.,-adams,-a.,-sharlet,-d.,-ragan-kelley,-j.,-&-fatahalian,-k.-\(2016\).-automatically-scheduling-halide-image-processing-pipelines.-acm-transactions-on-graphics-\(tog\),-35-\(4\),-1–11.)) is a metadata store for MLOps, developed by Neptune Labs. It is designed to keep track of machine learning experiments, ensuring better collabo-

ration, reproducibility, and transparency in research. Neptune allows researchers to log and organize experiments, compare different model versions, and visualize metrics and parameters in real time. This is crucial for managing the complexity of [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) projects, where numerous experiments with various hyperparameters and configurations need to be tracked systematically.

[**MLIR**](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,)

[MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) (Lattner et al., [2021](#lattner,-c.,-&-adve,-v.-\(2004\).-llvm:-a-compilation-framework-for-lifelong-program-analysis-&-transformation.-proceedings-of-the-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization:-feedback-directed-and-runtime-optimization,-75.)) is a compiler infrastructure project by [LLVM](#llvm-low-level-virtual-machine-13,-40,-43,-63) (Lattner & Adve, [2004](#krizhevsky,-a.,-sutskever,-i.,-&-hinton,-g.-e.-\(2012\).-imagenet-classification-with-deep-convolutional-neural-networks.-advances-in-neural-information-processing-sys--tems,-1097–1105.)), aimed at reducing the complexity of developing machine learning frameworks and other domain-specific compilers. [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) provides a common intermediate representa- tion that can be used to optimize and transform code at multiple levels of abstraction. It supports custom dialects and transformations, making it highly extensible and suit- able for optimizing high-performance code, such as the one generated in machine learning workflows.

#### [**MLIR**](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) **Python Bindings**

[MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) Python bindings (Lattner et al., [2021](#lattner,-c.,-&-adve,-v.-\(2004\).-llvm:-a-compilation-framework-for-lifelong-program-analysis-&-transformation.-proceedings-of-the-international-symposium-on-code-generation-and-optimization:-feedback-directed-and-runtime-optimization,-75.)) allow Python developers to interact with the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) framework directly from Python code. These bindings provide a way to script and automate the compilation and optimization processes within the Python environ- ment, leveraging the power of [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) without needing to delve into C++ code. This is particularly useful in our research, as it integrates seamlessly with other Python-based tools and libraries, enabling efficient development and testing of compiler optimizations and [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) models.

These tools collectively support the various aspects of our research, from developing and training [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) models to managing experiments and optimizing code execution perfor- mance.

2. ## [**MLIR**](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) **Python [API](#api-application-programming-interface-60,-63,-70)** {#mlir-python-api}

Since all the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) work will be done in Python, we need to establish a connection between Python and the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler. For example, Python should instruct the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler on which transformations to apply, and [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) should report back to Python with the execution time or loop features of a specific Linalg operation. Although there is an existing Python [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) binding, it is currently immature and limited. It cannot be used to apply optimization transformations, works only for very specific cases, and is compatible only with earlier versions of [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,), not the latest version. So, to achieve this connection between Python and [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,), we used two methods:

1. **Establishing a Connection Between Python and the C++ Interface of [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,):** We communicate between Python and C++ using command lines and

   shared files. By using Python to build customized commands and execute them with the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler binaries, we can perform transformations on a specific [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) file.

2. **Using the Transform Dialect:** The Transform dialect is responsible for perform- ing various transformations on [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code, such as lowering from one dialect to an- other, canonicalization, and applying optimizations like Tiling, Tiling+Parallelization, Interchange, and Vectorization. This is done by appending a Transform dialect script to the end of the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code and applying the transformation using the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler. We exploit this feature by using Python code to design specific Transform dialect scripts, adding them to the end of the current [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code, and then applying the transformation. This enables us to control the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code within the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment using just Python code.

   Now we’re able to do the following:

* Apply Tiling and Tiling+Parallelization with any tile sizes to a specific Linalg op- eration.

* Apply Interchange with any permutation array to a specific Linalg operation.

* Apply Vectorization to a specific Linalg operation.

* Target a specific Linalg operation and lower it into the Affine dialect to retrieve its loop information.

* Isolate a specific Linalg operation, wrap it inside a function, and replace it with a function call.

* Identify the producers and consumers of a specific Linalg operation.

* Build the computational graph of an [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code.

* Obtain the execution time of [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) codes.

* Return the transformed [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code.

* Fuse specific consumers with specific producers.

  3. ## **Environment** {#environment-1}

In this section, we delve into the design of the environment for our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) solution. The environment plays a critical role in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) as it provides the context within which the agent operates, receives observations, and takes actions. Here, we detail the implementation of the environment, focusing on two key functions: the step function and the reset function.

1. ### **Step Function** {#step-function-1}

The step function is at the heart of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment, facilitating the interaction be- tween the agent and the environment. This function takes an action as input, applies the corresponding transformation to the current state, and updates the environment accord- ingly. Please check Algorithm [4](#algorithm-4-step-function) for a pseudo-code for the reset function.

**Algorithm 4** Step Function

1: **function** step(self, action)

2:	state \= self.state

3:	transformation, parameters \= **decode\_action**(action)

4:	new\_code \= **apply\_transformation**(state.code, transformation, parameters)

5:	**if** transformation \== "Vectorization" **then**

6:	new\_exec\_time \= **execute\_code**(new\_code)

7:	speedup \= self.exec\_time / new\_exec\_time

8:	reward \= speedup

9:	**else**

10:	reward \= 0

11:	**end if**

12:	new\_state \= state.copy()	▷ We update the state of the environment

13:	new\_state.code \= new\_code

14:	new\_state.action\_history.append(action)

15:	new\_state.rewards.append(reward)

16:	self.state \= new\_state

17:		observation	\=	build\_observation\_vector(new\_state.loops\_info, new\_state.action\_history)  
18:	**return** observation, reward

 19: **end function**	

The step function begins by decoding the action to identify the transformation and its parameters. It then applies this transformation to the current code. If the transfor- mation is "Vectorization", the function executes the transformed code to measure the new execution time, calculates the speedup, and sets the reward accordingly. For other transformations, the reward is set to zero. The state is then updated with the new code, action history, and rewards. Finally, the function builds the observation vector from the updated state and returns this observation along with the reward.

2. ### **Reset Function** {#reset-function-1}

The reset function initializes the environment at the start of a new episode. It randomly selects a Linalg operation code, retrieves its loop information and features to build the representation vector, executes the code to get the execution time, and sets up a new state. Please check Algorithm [5](#algorithm-5-reset-function) for a pseudo-code for the reset function.

The reset function plays a crucial role in preparing the environment for a new episode. It ensures that each episode starts with a fresh state, which is critical for the agent’s

**Algorithm 5** Reset Function

1: **function** reset(self)

2:	code \= get\_random\_code(self.linalg\_operations)

3:	loops\_info \= get\_loops\_info(code) 4:	exec\_time \= execute\_code(code) 5:  
6:	state \= **new** State()

7:	state.code \= code

8:  state.loops\_info \= loops\_info 9:  state.exec\_time \= exec\_time 10:  state.action\_history \= \[ \]  
11:   state.rewards \= \[ \]

12:

13:	self.state \= state

14:

15:	observation \= build\_observation\_vector(state.loops\_info, state.action\_history)

16:	**return** observation

 17: **end function**	

learning process. The function builds the observation vector from the new state and returns it, setting the stage for the agent to begin interacting with the environment.

By carefully designing these functions, we create a robust and flexible environment that supports the training and evaluation of our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent. This setup allows the agent to explore various transformations, evaluate their impact on code performance, and learn optimal strategies for code optimization.

4. ## **Training Algorithms** {#training-algorithms}

In this section, we explore two fundamental [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) algorithms, namely Proximal Policy Op- timization (PPO) and Deep Q-Network (DQN), within the context of our solution. PPO is a state-of-the-art policy gradient method that has gained prominence in recent years for its stability and robustness in training deep neural network policies. It operates by iteratively collecting trajectories from the environment and updating the policy to max- imize expected rewards while constraining policy changes. On the other hand, DQN is a classic algorithm that combines Q-learning with deep neural networks to approximate the action-value function, enabling efficient learning in large state spaces. By comparing the performance and characteristics of PPO and DQN in optimizing compiler code using our designed environment, we aim to gain insights into the strengths and limitations of each algorithm.

1. ### **Proximal Policy Optimization** {#proximal-policy-optimization}

PPO generally consists of two phases: the trajectory collection phase and the model update phase.

In the trajectory collection phase (As shown in Algorithm [6](#algorithm-6-collect-trajectories)), we gather tuples of (state, action, reward) from the environment. In this problem, the state is the observation vector of the code, the action includes the transformation applied along with its parameters and the probability of that action, and the reward is either 0 if the episode didn’t end or the acceleration if the episode ended.

The second phase is the model update phase (As shown in Algorithm [7](#algorithm-7-update-model)), where the collected trajectories are used to calculate the loss for updating the model. This involves using the updated model to recalculate the probabilities of the collected actions from the collected states and comparing them to the old action probabilities by calculating the ratio of their probabilities. This provides a measure of the extent of the model’s update. Additionally, the Generalized Advantage Estimation (GAE) of the rewards is calculated using the value estimated by the value network. We then compute the non- clipped surrogate loss (surr1) and the clipped surrogate loss (surr2) using a clip factor ε of  
0.2. This clipping helps stabilize and smooth the training process. We also calculate the mean squared error (MSE) loss between the predicted value of the state and the estimated target value. Finally, we sum both the policy loss and value loss, backpropagate the total loss, and update the model’s parameters.

**Algorithm 6** Collect Trajectories

1:    **function**  Collect\_Trajectories(length\_trajectories)

2:	trajectories \= \[ \]

3:  Env \= **new** Environment()		▷ Create an environment instance 4:  state \= Env.reset()	▷ Reset the environment to get the initial state 5:  **for** each i in 0 to length\_trajectories **do**  
6:	action \= **model.policy**(state)

7:	next\_state, reward \= Env.step(action)

8:	**if** action **is** Vectorization **then** ▷ Vectorization indicates the end of an episode

9:	state \= Env.reset()

10:	**else**

11:	state \= next\_state

12:	**end if**

13:	trajectories.append((state, action, reward))

14:	**end for**

15:	**return** trajectories	▷ Return the collected trajectories

 16: **end function**	

5. ## **Policy Network** {#policy-network}

In this section we detail the implementation of the policy network for both Hierarchical Action Space and Unrolled Action Space.

**Algorithm 7** Update Model

1: **function** Update\_Model(trajectories, model, critic, optimizer, critic\_optimizer, gamma, epsilon)  
2:	**for** each (state, action, reward, next\_state) **in** trajectories **do**

3:	new\_action \= **model.policy**(state)

4:	advantage \= GAE(reward, state, next\_state, critic, gamma)

5:	ratio \= action.log\_p / new\_action.log\_p

6:	surr\_1 \= ratio \* advantage	▷ Surrogate loss 1 7:	surr\_2 \= clip(ratio, 1 − ε, 1 \+ ε) \* advantage	▷ Surrogate loss 2 8:	policy\_loss \= **mean**( **min**(surr\_1, surr\_2) )

9:

10:	target\_value \= reward \+ gamma \* **model.critic**(next\_state).detach()

11:	current\_value \= **model.critic**(state)  
12:	value\_loss \= **mean**( (current\_value \- target\_value)2 )

13:	loss \= policy\_loss \+ 0.5 x value\_loss

14:

15:	optimizer.zero\_grad()			▷ Zero the gradients 16:	policy\_loss.backward()	▷ Perform backpropagation for policy loss 17:	optimizer.step()		▷ Update the model parameters 18:  **end for**

 19: **end function**	

1. ### **Policy Network using Hierarchical Action Space** {#policy-network-using-hierarchical-action-space}

Partitioning the action space into the Cartesian product of smaller action spaces and representing it as a Hierarchical Action Space necessitated an adaptation of our policy network. As shown in Algorithm [8](#algorithm-8-policy-forward-function-using-hierarchical-action-space), it first passes the observation through a backbone network to extract relevant features. Then, it samples a transformation and its corre- sponding probability distribution from the output of the backbone network. Depending on the sampled transformation, different sub-networks are employed to further process the features. For instance, if the transformation is Tiling or Tiling+Parallelization, the [T](#t-tiling-49,-50,-53,-68) network or the [TP](#tp-tiling-+-parallelization-49,-50,-53,-68) network is utilized, respectively, to sample the tile sizes. Similarly, for Interchange, the Interchange network determines the permutation index. Finally, for Vectorization, no additional processing is required. The function returns the sampled action along with its probability, which is used during the training process. This mecha- nism ensures that the model dynamically selects the appropriate action to optimize the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code based on the observed state. Also note that the shapes of each output of the employed sub-networks are significantly smaller compared to the overall length of the action space, which is orders of magnitude larger.

2. ### **Policy Network using Unrolled Action Space** {#policy-network-using-unrolled-action-space}

In this method, as shown in Algorithm [9](#algorithm-9-policy-forward-function-using-unrolled-action-space), we simply use one backbone network that takes the observation vector of the code and outputs a size equal to the size of the Unrolled Action Space, which contains all possible actions. We then sample one action from this output. This action contains both the transformation to be applied and its parameters.

**Algorithm 8** Policy Forward Function using Hierarchical Action Space

1: **function** forward(obs)	▷ Takes observation vector as input

2:

3:    x ← backbone(obs)  
4:   transformation, transformation\_p ← x.sample()

5:

#### 6:   **if transformation is "Tiling" then**

7:		x ← T\_network(x)		▷ shape \== N x (M \+ 1\) 8:	x ← reshape(x, (N, M \+ 1))	▷ shape \== N x (N, M \+ 1\) 9:	tiling\_sizes, tiling\_sizes\_p ← x.sample()			▷ shape \== N 10:		proba ← transformation\_p x tiling\_sizes\_p.prod()  
11:	action ← (("Tiling", tiling\_sizes), proba)

12:

#### 13:   **else if transformation is "Tiling+Parallelization" then**

14:	x ← TP\_network(x)		▷ shape \== N x (M \+ 1\) 15:	x ← reshape(x, (N, M \+ 1))	▷ shape \== N x (N, M \+ 1\) 16:	tiling\_sizes, tiling\_sizes\_p ← x.sample()			▷ shape \== N 17:	proba ← transformation\_p x tiling\_sizes\_p.prod()  
18:	action ← (("Tiling+Parallelization", tiling\_sizes), proba)

19:

#### 20:   **else if transformation is "Interchange" then**

21:	x ← Interchange\_network(x)	▷ shape \== N 22:	permutation\_idx, permutation\_p ← x.sample()		▷ shape \== 1 23:	proba ← transformation\_p x permutation\_p  
24:	action ← (("Interchange", permutation\_idx), proba)

25:

#### 26:	**else if transformation is "Vectorization" then**

27:	proba ← transformation\_p

28:	action ← (("Vectorization", ), proba)

29:	**end if**

30:

31:	**return** action, proba

 32: **end function**	

Despite the simplicity of such a policy network, the large size of the output can pose a problem to the training algorithms as it becomes harder to explore it efficiently.

**Algorithm 9** Policy Forward Function using Unrolled Action Space

1: **function** forward(obs)	▷ Takes observation vector as input

2:

3:	x ← backbone(obs)  
4:	action, proba ← x.sample()	▷ action \== xxxxx

5:

6:	**return** action, proba

 7: **end function**	

6. ## **Conclusion** {#conclusion-4}

In this section, we have delved into the foundational components of our solution, encom- passing the tools, the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) Python [API](#api-application-programming-interface-60,-63,-70), the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment, and the policy networks. Through the utilization of tools like Torch and Neptune, we laid the groundwork for im- plementing our solution efficiently and effectively. The [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) Python [API](#api-application-programming-interface-60,-63,-70) provided a bridge between Python and the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler, enabling seamless communication and interaction. Our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) environment, meticulously designed and optimized, served as the stage for our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) experiments, facilitating the collection of trajectories and the training of our agents. Lastly, the policy networks, tailored to handle both Unrolled and Hier- archical Action Spaces, played a pivotal role in guiding the decision-making process of our agents. Together, these components form the backbone of our solution, providing the framework necessary to tackle the challenging task of automatic code optimization in the [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) compiler.

# **Chapter 6** {#chapter-6}

**Experiments and Results**

In this section, we present the experiments conducted to evaluate the effectiveness of our proposed [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,)\-based approach for optimizing [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code. Our primary objective is to compare the performance of various methods against traditional heuristics and baseline approaches in terms of execution time, speedup, and overall efficiency. We detail the experimental setup, including the hardware and software environments, and the specific configurations for the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent.

We begin by describing the benchmark suite employed to test our optimization strate- gies, ensuring a diverse set of applications to comprehensively evaluate the effectiveness generalization of our approach. Additionally, we discuss the training process for the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent, including the selection of hyperparameters, the reward structures implemented, and the convergence criteria. The results are then presented, highlighting the comparative per- formance of different strategies, including [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) agent, [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) agent, immediate rewards, final rewards, etc . . . We analyze the impact of each strategy on the overall performance improvements, providing detailed charts and graphs to visualize the results. Finally, we discuss the implications of our findings, noting any significant trends, limitations, and potential areas for future work.

1. ## **Experimental Setup** {#experimental-setup}

We aim to test the effectiveness of our approach in two main setups: Single Opeeration Optimization and Whole Program Optimization.

1. ### **Single Operation Optimization** {#single-operation-optimization}

In this setup, we focus on optimizing individual Linalg operations. We believe that improving the performance of individual operations will significantly enhance the overall performance of [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) scientific code. Therefore, we isolate [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) operations and aim to find the best schedules for each one. To gradually test the effectiveness of our approach, we proceed as follows:

1) **Training on One Single Operation** This is the most basic setup where the goal is to train the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent on a single operation and determine if it can find an optimal schedule for that operation. To evaluate the agent’s performance, we compare the final speedup to the speedup achieved through manual scheduling and heuristic methods, such as Beam Search. This initial experiment is a crucial milestone, as optimizing entire programs requires the ability to optimize individual operations first.

2) **Training on One Type of Operation** In this second step, the goal is to train the agent to generalize over a specific type of operation, such as matrix multiplication (Mat- mul), 2D convolution, and 2D max pooling. After analyzing numerous neural network architectures, we identified these operations as the most common in neural networks, and optimizing them would lead to overall network optimization. The objective is to see if our agent can learn the necessary knowledge to generalize across any operation within a specific type.

3) **Training One Agent to Generalize Over Multiple Types of Operations** In this final step, we aim to train a neural network that can generalize over multiple types of Linalg operations, including matmul, 2D convolution, and 2D max pooling. To expand the scope, we also adapt our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) system to accept generic operations, covering a broader range of operations.

   2. ### **Whole Program Optimization** {#whole-program-optimization}

In contrast to the previous experiments, where the focus was on optimizing single oper- ations, we now aim to train an agent to find the best schedules for programs containing two or more operations. This setup tests the agent’s ability to handle the complexity of optimizing multiple interconnected operations within a complete program.

2. ## **Evaluation Environment** {#evaluation-environment}

All the experiments are conducted on a high-performance computing (HPC) cluster utiliz- ing the SLURM workload manager. The hardware setup comprises a single node equipped with 28 CPU cores allocated specifically for the task. The node has a memory capacity of 64 gigabytes, ensuring enough memory for intensive compilations.

3. ## **Training on One Single Operation** {#training-on-one-single-operation}

We believe that this initial experiment is crucial to the overall project as it represents the foundation and will determine which configurations to use in further experiments. Therefore, we dedicated a significant amount of focus to this part. Our goal is to compare

different configurations and identify the most effective one. Specifically, we aim to evaluate the following comparisons:

* [**PPO**](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) **vs [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76)** : These are two prominent [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) algorithms with distinct approaches to learning and optimization. By comparing [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) and [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76), we aim to identify which algorithm provides better performance, faster convergence, and more robust results in the context of our specific tasks.

* **"Immediate Reward" vs "Final Reward"**: The reward function plays a critical role in [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,). The "Immediate Reward" provides feedback after each action, while the "Final Reward" gives feedback only at the end of an episode. Comparing these reward structures will help us understand their impact on learning efficiency and effectiveness in optimizing schedules.

* **Unrolled Action Space vs Hierarchical Action Space**: The representation of the action space can significantly influence the learning process. An unrolled action space treats each possible action as a separate entity, while a hierarchical action space breaks down actions into smaller, more manageable components. By comparing these approaches, we aim to determine which method facilitates better exploration and exploitation of the action space, leading to more optimal scheduling solutions.

Through these comparisons, we seek to establish a robust framework for our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) model, ensuring that we select the most effective configurations for subsequent experiments.

1. ### **Proximal Policy Optimization ([PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,)) vs Deep Q-Network ([DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76))** {#proximal-policy-optimization-(ppo)-vs-deep-q-network-(dqn)}

[PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) and [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) are both well-known [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) algorithms with significant impacts on the field of AI. We decided to test both training algorithms and compare them in terms of overall performance (i.e., which one results in better speedup), convergence speed, complexity of experiments, and transferability to other tasks.

#### **Experimental Setup**

In this experiment, we train two agents: one using [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) and the other using [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) . We train both agents on a matrix multiplication (matmul) operation, multiplying a matrix of shape (1200x1500) by a matrix of shape (1500x1000). We also train them on a 2D convolution operation, where the input tensor has a shape of 32x230x230x3 (32 is the batch size, 230 is the height and width of the input, and 3 is the number of channels). The filter used has a shape of 7x7x3x64 (7 is the height and width of the kernel, and 64 is the number of filters).

For both training algorithms, we use the same policy network with an Unrolled Action Space because [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) does not support the multi-action policy network required for a

Hierarchical Action Space. The table below provides detailed information about both training algorithms.

#### **Hyperparameter	Value**

#### **Results**

We observe in Fig. [6.1](#comparison-of-ppo-and-dqn-on-matmul-\(1200x1500\)-*-\(1500x1000\)-comparison-of-ppo-and-dqn-on-cconv2d-32x230x230x3-*-7x7x3x64) and Fig. [6.2](#comparison-of-ppo-and-dqn-on-matmul-\(1200x1500\)-*-\(1500x1000\)-comparison-of-ppo-and-dqn-on-cconv2d-32x230x230x3-*-7x7x3x64) that [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) outperforms [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) for both the matrix multiplication (matmul) and 2D convolution operations. [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) shows a significantly faster convergence compared to [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76), which requires many more steps to begin converging. This rapid convergence of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) can be attributed to its more effective use of learned knowl- edge even in the early stages of training, allowing it to quickly optimize the schedules and achieve higher performance. Additionally, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) consistently achieves much higher speedups than [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76), indicating its superior ability to find and exploit optimal transfor- mation sequences.

By the end of the training, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) converges to a fixed speedup with minimal fluctu- ations, demonstrating stable and reliable performance. In contrast, [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) still exhibits

aggressive spikes and fluctuations in speedup, indicating instability and inconsistency in its optimization process. These fluctuations in [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76)’s performance suggest that it strug- gles to maintain and build upon the useful knowledge it acquires, likely due to its less effective handling of exploration and exploitation phases.

To check the stability of the [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) model, we repeated the same experiment with different random initialization seeds. The results shown in Fig. [6.3](#dqn-experiments-on-matmul-across-varying-seeds) demonstrate that the same agent, when trained on three different random seeds, shows varying behavior. The agents do not converge at the same speed, nor do they converge to the same speedup, indicating less stability and reliability.

#### **Interpretation**

The [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) algorithm relies on an exploration-exploitation strategy that initially forces the model to explore the action space before gradually shifting to exploiting the learned knowledge. As a result, even if the agent discovers an effective schedule early in the training, it cannot exploit this schedule in subsequent episodes because it is still required to take random actions until the exploration phase concludes. This can cause the valuable knowledge acquired by the model to fade away before the exploitation phase begins. Additionally, it is challenging to determine the appropriate value for the exploration rate ε in advance, making it difficult to decide how long the agent should explore before transitioning to exploitation.

In contrast, [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) does not employ such a naive exploration-exploitation strategy. Useful knowledge can be exploited from the very beginning of the training, allowing the [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) model to quickly converge and achieve higher speedup rates. It is also worth noting that [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) struggles with large action spaces and often fails to exploit them effectively, unlike [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,), which handles them better.

#### **Conclusion**

Overall, the results demonstrate the advantages of [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) over [DQN](#dqn-deep-q-learning-23,-25,-26,-30,-71,-73–76) in optimizing single operations, highlighting [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,)’s robustness and efficiency in handling large action spaces.

2. ### **Immediate Reward vs Final Reward** {#immediate-reward-vs-final-reward}

Here, we experiment with two reward functions. The "Immediate Reward" returns the speedup obtained for each optimization applied, while the "Final Reward" returns a reward of 0 until the end of the episode (indicating the end of the schedule), where we then return the overall speedup of the optimized program.

In order to check the effect of the reward function, we experimented with two different reward functions: "Immediate Reward" and "Final Reward". For this, we trained two agents using the [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) algorithm with the exact, same parameters, focusing on a matrix

multiplication operation. Both agents were trained for the same number of iterations over the operation.

#### **Results**

Comparison of PPO with Final Reward and Immediate Reward

1200

1000

800

600

400

200

Iteration

Figure 6.4: The evolution of the speedup over training iterations using "Final Reward" and "Immediate Reward".

Comparison of PPO with Final Reward and Immediate Reward

1400

1200

1000

800

600

400

200

0h	1h	2h	3h	4h	5h	6h	7h	8h	9h	10h 11h

Training time

Figure 6.5: The evolution of the speedup over time using "Final Reward" and "Immediate Reward"

As depicted in Fig. [6.4](#comparison-of-ppo-with-final-reward-and-immediate-reward), we notice that both reward functions yield very compara- ble results, with "Final Reward" demonstrating slightly faster convergence compared to "Immediate Reward". There is also a slight increase in the final speedup for "Final Re- ward" over "Immediate Reward", but this difference is negligible and does not indicate a significant performance gap.

Fig. [6.5](#comparison-of-ppo-with-final-reward-and-immediate-reward-1) shows that [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) with "Final Reward" is much faster than [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) with "Im- mediate Reward". "Final Reward" takes around 2 hours to build approximately 7000 schedules, whereas "Immediate Reward" takes more than 10 hours to achieve the same. This is because the latter evaluates intermediate states of the code before completing the schedule, which is very time-consuming and slows down the entire training process.

#### **Interpretation**

The results indicate that both reward functions produce similar performance in terms of speedup. The slight advantage in convergence speed and final speedup of the "Final Reward" function over the "Immediate Reward" function is not substantial enough to suggest a significant performance gap.

However, the notable difference lies in the efficiency of the training process. The "Final Reward" function’s approach of evaluating only the final state of the schedule significantly reduces the training time, making it more practical for large-scale experiments. This efficiency makes the "Final Reward" method more suitable for our purposes, as it allows for quicker iterations and the ability to handle more schedules within a given time frame. Consequently, we will adopt the "Final Reward" method for all subsequent experiments due to its superior training efficiency.

#### **Conclusion**

In conclusion, both reward functions produce comparable results. Despite "Final Reward" only evaluating the last state of the code after the end of the schedule, it can acquire meaningful knowledge to find optimal schedules. This makes the "Final Reward" method more efficient, and we will continue to use it in all subsequent experiments.

3. ### **Unrolled Action Space vs Hierarchical Action Space** {#unrolled-action-space-vs-hierarchical-action-space}

In the design of our solution, we developed two possible representations of the action space, as the action space in automatic code optimization can exponentially increase and become intractable. We conducted experiments using [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) with an Unrolled Action Space, which represents all possible transformations and hyperparameters in a list, to select the best action from that list. This method is the easiest to implement. We also conducted experiments using [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) with a Hierarchical Action Space, where the overall action space is represented as the product of multiple smaller action spaces. Note that these experiments use different policy networks adapted to their respective action spaces. In the Unrolled Action Space, the policy network selects one action from a list, while in the Hierarchical Action Space, the policy network selects multiple actions, including the type of transformation and the parameters for each loop to construct the final action.

Comparison of PPO with Final Reward and Immediate Reward

1400

1200

1000

800

600

400

200

0

0	1000	2000	3000	4000	5000	6000	7000

Iterations

Figure 6.6: The evolution of the speedup using Hierarchical Action Space and Unrolled Action Space

#### **Results**

We first notice that the method using Hierarchical Action Space has a slower convergence compared to Unrolled Action Space, which quickly achieved a high speedup. In the early stages of training, the Unrolled method demonstrated rapid performance improvements, quickly reaching significant speedup levels. However, as iterations progressed, the Hierar- chical method continued to increase its speedup incrementally, eventually surpassing the Unrolled method after approximately 4000 iterations. Following this point, the Hierarchi- cal method maintained a steady increase in performance, ultimately reaching a speedup of 1510, where it converged and stabilized, maintaining this high-performance level until the end of the training period.

#### **Interpretation**

The key advantage of using a Hierarchical Action Space lies in its ability to cover a much larger portion of the entire action space of all possible combinations of actions. In contrast, the Unrolled Action Space is limited in its ability to encompass the full range of potential actions, as it is impractical to represent every possible combination in a flat list. As a result, the Hierarchical policy network can explore a broader and more diverse set of actions, which ultimately allows it to identify more effective optimization strategies. This extensive exploration capability is the primary reason why the Hierarchical method eventually converges to a higher speedup.

The slower convergence observed in the Hierarchical method can be attributed to the complexity and size of the action space. The hierarchical agent must carefully navigate this vast space, exploring numerous combinations and sequences of actions to identify the most effective schedules. This careful and thorough exploration process enables the hierarchical agent to uncover highly optimized schedules that might be missed by the unrolled method. Consequently, the initial slower convergence is offset by the eventual

discovery of superior optimization strategies, leading to higher overall performance.

#### **Conclusion**

In conclusion, despite the complexity of the policy network, using a Hierarchical Action Space is better than using an Unrolled Action Space. It allows the agent to explore more actions without having to sample from a distribution over a large list of actions. Instead, it carefully selects each transformation and its hyperparameters one by one.

4. ## **Training on One Type of Operations** {#training-on-one-type-of-operations}

Previously, we aimed to train an agent to find the best schedule for optimizing one op- eration at a time. The goal was to identify the optimal configuration of the learning algorithm, action space representation, and reward function. This approach made it eas- ier to experiment with a single operation. We concluded that the best configuration is to use [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) as the learning algorithm, employ a Hierarchical Action Space, and utilize a reward function that returns zero except at the end of the schedule, where it returns the final achieved speedup.

In this second category of experiments, we aim to expand our focus from single op- erations to entire types of operations. Specifically, we target matrix multiplications and 2D convolutions, as these represent a significant portion of operations in scientific code, especially in neural networks. The goal is to determine if our agent can generalize over a set of operations within the same class. During the training, we don’t have a fixed-size dataset, but instead, we randomly generate operations on the fly with random shapes for more diversity in the training data.

#### **Experimental Setup**

We maintain the same setup as before, using a hierarchical policy network trained with [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) and a final speedup reward. To evaluate our method, we train two agents using this configuration. The first agent is trained on matrix multiplication (matmul) operations, and the second agent is trained on 2D convolution (conv2d) operations. The agents are trained on operations with randomly generated shapes at each reset of the environment. To verify the generalization capability of the model, we evaluate its performance on unseen operations.

#### **Results and Interpretation**

Figures [6.7](#the-speedup-of-matmul-operations) and [6.8](#the-speedup-of-conv2d-operations) show that both models successfully find effective schedules for both matmul and conv2d operations. For matmul, the agent achieves a speedup of up to 27000x, and for conv2d, a speedup of 108x is reached. We also observe that some oper- ations are optimized better than others within both types. This suggests that the more computationally expensive the operation, the greater the potential for optimization.

#### **Conclusion**

These results demonstrate that the agent is capable of acquiring the necessary knowledge to optimize an entire type of operations effectively. The agent not only excels in finding optimal schedules for the operations it was trained on but also generalizes well to unseen operations within the same type. This ability to generalize indicates that the agent has developed a robust understanding of the optimization process for these types of oper- ations, making it a versatile tool for improving performance in various scenarios. The success in optimizing matrix multiplications and 2D convolutions, which are prevalent in scientific computing and neural networks, underscores the potential of our method to further generalize on other operations.

5. ## **Training One Agent to Generalize Over Multiple Types of Operations** {#training-one-agent-to-generalize-over-multiple-types-of-operations}

Finally, after experimenting on isolated individual operations and then on groups of op- erations, we now aim to train an agent on multiple types of operations. This represents one of the ultimate goals of our method: to create a versatile and robust agent capable of optimizing a diverse range of operations within a single framework.

Our previous experiments have shown promising results in optimizing specific oper- ations and groups of related operations. Building on this success, we now seek to push the boundaries further by integrating multiple types of operations into a unified training regimen. This approach will allow us to evaluate the agent’s ability to generalize across different types of operations and enhance its adaptability to various optimization tasks.

1. ### **Experimental Setup** {#experimental-setup-1}

For this set of experiments, we will maintain the same configuration that has proven effective in previous trials. This includes using [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) as the learning algorithm, employing a Hierarchical Action Space, and utilizing a reward function that returns zero except at the end of the schedule, where it reflects the final achieved speedup.

By training the agent on multiple operation types including matrix multiplications, 2D convolutions, 2D maxpooling, addition, and many other generic operations, we aim to observe the agent’s performance across these diverse operations. The ultimate goal is to ensure that our method can be applied broadly and effectively, making it a powerful tool for optimizing complex scientific and machine learning code. Once again, we do not

use a fixed-size training dataset, but instead, we randomly generate operations on the fly during training.

Through these experiments, we hope to demonstrate that our approach is not only effective for specific operations but also scalable and generalizable across multiple types, thereby validating its utility and robustness in real-world applications.

In some cases, the agent achieves even better speedups than the specific-type agents. For example, when the general agent optimizes certain matrix multiplications or convolution operations, it sometimes surpasses the speedup achieved by agents trained exclusively on those operations.

However, it shows a low speedup in generic operations, with some having a speedup very close to 1 (indicating no speedup) and some even lower than 1 (indicating that these operations are now slower).

maxpool 2d: 32x64x112x112 | 2x2

Speedup

Figure 6.11: The speedup of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent on other sets of operations.

3. ### **Interpretation** {#interpretation}

The ability of the general agent to perform comparably or even better than the specific- type agents suggests that it has successfully acquired and leveraged generalized knowledge from the diverse operations. This indicates that the agent is not merely memorizing specific optimizations but is learning underlying principles that can be applied across different types of operations.

We’ve also noticed that we get the biggest speedup when operations are very com- putationally expensive, and this speedup decreases as the computational demands of the operations decrease, generally by reducing the shapes of their input matrices. For the case of max pooling, it’s not as computationally expensive as matrix multiplications (matmuls) and convolutions, because it’s just sliding a window over a matrix and taking the maximum of that window. Therefore, it’s understandable that the speedup is not as significant as in matmul and conv2d. The same goes for addition and subtraction, which are operations of lower complexity.

For generic operations, we noticed an even lower speedup. This is due to these opera- tions being not computationally intensive at all. They are mostly used in neural networks for reshaping, applying activation functions like [ReLU](#relu-rectified-linear-unit-55,-87,-91) (which involves taking the maxi- mum between a value and 0), and softmax functions (which involves mapping values using an exponential function and division). Thus, it’s understandable why we don’t achieve high speedups for these operations. In cases where we get a speedup lower than 1 for generic operations, we believe this is due to certain forced assumptions in the optimiza- tion process, such as mandating vectorization at the end of the schedule, which does not necessarily work well for operations with very low levels of computation.

Ovverall, the improved performance in some instances highlights the advantage of training on multiple operation types at once, where insights gained from one type of op- erations can inform and enhance the optimization of another. This adaptability demon- strates the robustness of the hierarchical action space and [PPO](#ppo-proximal-policy-optimization-23,-27–30,-32,-37,-44–46,-55,-57,-58,-61,-71,-73–76,) approach, as it allows the agent to navigate a larger and more complex action space effectively.

4. ### **Conclusion** {#conclusion-5}

Consequently, the agent’s ability to generalize and transfer knowledge across multiple types of operations significantly shows its applicability and efficiency in optimizing various computational tasks.

6. ## **Comparaison with Beam Search and Exhaustive Search** {#comparaison-with-beam-search-and-exhaustive-search}

In this section, we compare our trained [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent against two other methods: Beam Search and Exhaustive Search.

**Beam Search** Beam Search is a heuristic search algorithm that explores a tree of possible solutions, expanding the most promising nodes. In the context of this solution, we use Beam Search with a beam size of 3\. This means that at each step t, we select the top 3 optimizations that yield the highest speedup and continue searching for further optimizations at the next step t \+ 1. In this setup, we do not impose any constraints on the action space, allowing the beam to determine the most advantageous branches to explore.

**Exhaustive Search** Exhaustive Search is a brute-force method that explores all possi- ble combinations in a given search space to find the optimal solution. Given the vastness of the action space in this context, we limit the search to a subset by restricting the tile sizes and possible interchanges. This makes the exhaustive search feasible by narrowing down the number of potential actions.

Comparison between the speedups of the RL Agent, Beam Search and Exhaustive Search
