1. ### **Results** {#results-1}

As shown in Fig. [6.12](#comparison-between-the-speedups-of-the-rl-agent,-beam-search-and-exhaustive-search), we evaluated the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent, the Beam Search method, and the Exhaustive Search method on 5 benchmark operations with varying shapes. The results demonstrate that the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent consistently outperformed the other methods on these benchmark operations, often by a significant margin, particularly in large matrix multi- plications (matmul).

We also compared our [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent to TensorFlow XLA, which automatically optimizes TensorFlow operations. We ran the same operations with identical shapes to see which optimizer performed better. As shown in Fig. [6.13](#speedup-of-the-rl-agent-over-tensorflow-xla), the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent surpassed the XLA compiler in all matmul operations with a substantial speedup. However, it exhibited a slight drawback in convolution operations, where XLA optimized better.

2. ### **Interpretation** {#interpretation-1}

The superior performance of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent can be attributed to its ability to learn and generalize optimization strategies from the training data, allowing it to navigate the action space more effectively than the heuristic-based Beam Search and the constrained Exhaus- tive Search. The [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent’s approach leverages its trained model to identify optimal schedules, demonstrating its robustness and efficiency in handling complex operations.

When comparing the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent to TensorFlow XLA, the results show that while the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent excels in optimizing matmul operations, it still faces challenges in optimizing convolution operations. This suggests that further refinement of the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) model could enhance its performance in diverse operation types, potentially surpassing specialized optimizers like XLA in all aspects.

Overall, these comparisons highlight the [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent’s strong potential for automatic optimization in machine learning and scientific computing contexts, showcasing its effec- tiveness and adaptability in optimizing a variety of operations.

7. ## **Whole Program Optimization** {#whole-program-optimization-1}

In the previous experiments, we trained an agent that takes a single operation as input and predicts the best schedule for that input. The agent performed particularly well on high-computation operations like matrix multiplication (matmul) and convolutions. The current goal is to expand this optimization beyond single operations to entire programs. This means the agent must now consider all operations in a program, or at least the surrounding operations, rather than focusing on one at a time.

A key feature to achieve this broader optimization is the introduction of a new trans- formation called “Fusion”. Fusion was not possible in the single-operation setup because it involves combining two operations that share loops. To implement Fusion, the agent must analyze operations that are connected, specifically those in a producer-consumer

relationship.

In the following, we will experiment on small neural networks and gradually scale up bigger ones. As shown in Fig. [6.14](#heading=h.b2650bwibzof), we begin by taking the Abstract Syntax Tree (AST) of the neural network in ([MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,)). In this representation, each Linalg operation corresponds to a node in the AST, and each edge signifies a producer-consumer relationship. Specifically, if an edge exists from node 1 to node 2, it indicates that node 1 is the producer of node  
2\. This relationship implies the potential to fuse node 1 into node 2\.

Fig. [6.15](#heading=h.osh12m4cuyrz) illustrates the outcome of our fusion experiment. The [RL](#rl-reinforcement-learning-17,-20–23,-27,-29–35,-37,-39,-41,-42,-44–47,-52–54,-57–60,) agent demonstrates the ability to learn specific fusion patterns. For instance, it identifies the pattern of fusing convolution, max-pooling, and [ReLU](#relu-rectified-linear-unit-55,-87,-91) operations, as well as fusing matrix multiplication and addition operations, which collectively represent a feed-forward layer in deep learning architectures. We regard this as an excellent initial performance in the optimization of complete [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,) code sequences.

|  | Batch size | Image size | Depth (Number of conv blocks) | Speedup |
| ----- | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| Network 1 | 32 | 240x240 | 2 | 10.31 |
| Network 2 | 128 | 240x240 | 2 | 10.56 |
| Network 3 | 32 | 480x480 | 2 | 10.22 |
| Network 4 | 32 | 242x242 | 4 | 13.56 |
| Network 5 | 32 | 480x480 | 4 | 12.95 |

Table 6.3: Information concerning the 5 experimental networks.

To evaluate the effect of optimization on network speedup, we experimented with five different T networks, varying batch sizes, image shapes, and network depths. The detailed configurations of these networks are presented in Table [6.3](#batch-size). Our observations indicate that increasing batch size and image size (Networks 2 and 3 ) does not significantly impact the speedup compared to the baseline (Network 1). However, when employing a deeper model (Networks 4 and 5), we achieve a more substantial speedup.

1. ### **Interpretation** {#interpretation-2}

The results suggest that the optimization techniques applied are more effective for deeper networks than for changes in batch size or image shape. This may imply that deeper networks benefit more from the optimization due to their increased complexity and com- putational demands. Consequently, the performance improvements are more pronounced in scenarios where the model’s depth is a limiting factor in computation. This insight can guide future optimization efforts, emphasizing the importance of focusing on network depth to maximize speedup gains.

Figure 6.14: The Abstract Syntax Tree of a neural network in [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,).

Figure 6.15: The Abstract Syntax Tree of a neural network in [MLIR](#mlir-multi-level-intermidiate-representation-13,-14,-47–49,-51,-58–64,-68,-70,-71,).

2. ### **Conclusion** {#conclusion-6}

Optimizing a full network involves a comprehensive approach that targets various aspects of the network architecture and training process. Our findings demonstrate that while modifications in batch size and image shape yield minimal impact on speedup, substantial performance gains are realized through optimizing network depth. This indicates that deeper networks benefit significantly from optimization, due to their inherent complexity and greater computational demands.

# **General conclusion** {#general-conclusion}

In this work, we developed a comprehensive Reinforcement Learning ecosystem to opti- mize MLIR code. We present a novel formulation of the action space called Hierarchical Action Space, along with a suitable multi-action policy network that we call the Hier- archical Policy Network. This problem formulation and architecture proved efficient in optimizing MLIR operations due to their high ability to explore vast action spaces of possible schedules. We also implemented our own Python API for MLIR optimization, which we used for the RL environment. This API is a significant contribution to the community, as it can serve as an experimental playground for future work in training RL agents to optimize MLIR operations.

In our search for the best configuration, we explored various other configurations, such as the Unrolled Action Space that represents the action space as a flat list of all possible actions. We examined two reward functions: the first, named "Immediate Re- ward," returns the speedup after each applied optimization, and the second, named "Final Reward," returns the speedup only after the end of the schedule to evaluate the whole schedule once and for all.

During our experiments, we began by training our agent on one operation at a time to assess its ability to explore the vast action space and achieve optimal schedules. We then moved on to entire categories of operations to assess its ability to generalize over multiple operations. Finally, we trained the agent on multiple categories of operations, covering most of the operations from the MLIR Linalg dialect. We compared the per- formance of our model against heuristics like beam search and exhaustive search with a constrained search space, outperforming both in most cases by a significant margin. We also demonstrated considerable improvement against state-of-the-art compilers such as TensorFlow XLA, yielding very comparable results and sometimes even outperforming it in certain operations. Lastly, we transitioned to targeting full neural networks, succeeding in optimizing them as well.

Our work is just the beginning of a research path and can be further extended. We plan to include more transformations in the action space, such as unrolling and skewing, which are known to be effective in nested loops optimization. We also aim to scale up to larger networks, such as large language models, in order to optimize them.
